<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P_religion_world.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/31px-P_religion_world.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/P_religion_world.svg/62px-P_religion_world.svg.png 2x" width="31"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Religion" title="Portal:Religion">Religion portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Buddhism" title="Template:Buddhism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Buddhism" title="Template talk:Buddhism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Buddhism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Gautama Buddha</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_gautama_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-name_gautama-7">[note 4]</a></sup> (also <b>Siddhārtha Gautama</b>, <b>Siddhattha Gotama</b>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_siddharta_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-name_siddharta-8">[note 5]</a></sup> <b>Shakyamuni</b>, <b>Sakkamuni</b>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sakkamuni_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-sakkamuni-9">[note 6]</a></sup> and <b>The Buddha</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_the_buddha_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-name_the_buddha-10">[note 7]</a></sup>) was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascetic" title="Ascetic">ascetic</a> and spiritual teacher of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> who lived during the 6th or 5th century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin19985,_9,_10,_14_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin19985,_9,_10,_14-11">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong20011_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong20011-12">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder200045_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder200045-13">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dating_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-dating-14">[note 8]</a></sup> He was the founder of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and is revered by Buddhists as a <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood" title="Buddhahood">fully enlightened being</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuswell200382_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuswell200382-15">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin19988_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin19988-16">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodhi_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodhi-17">[note 9]</a></sup> who taught a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_paths_to_awakening" title="Buddhist paths to awakening">path</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">Nirvana</a> (lit. vanishing or extinguishing), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vimutti" title="Vimutti">freedom</a> from <a href="/wiki/Avidy%C4%81_(Buddhism)" title="Avidyā (Buddhism)">ignorance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Up%C4%81d%C4%81na" title="Upādāna">craving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra_(Buddhism)" title="Saṃsāra (Buddhism)">rebirth and suffering</a>. 
</p><p>According to Buddhist tradition, the Buddha was born in <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a> in what is now <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthplace_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-2">[note 2]</a></sup> to highborn parents of the <a href="/wiki/Shakya" title="Shakya">Shakya</a> clan, but <a href="/wiki/Great_Renunciation" title="Great Renunciation">abandoned</a> <a href="/wiki/Householder_(Buddhism)" title="Householder (Buddhism)">his family</a> to live as a wandering ascetic.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuswellLopez2014entry_"Sakyamuni"_18-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuswellLopez2014entry_"Sakyamuni"-18'>[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buswell_Lopez_renunciation_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buswell_Lopez_renunciation-19">[note 10]</a></sup> Leading a life of <a href="/wiki/Begging" title="Begging">begging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a>, and meditation, he attained <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Buddhism" title="Enlightenment in Buddhism">enlightenment</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>. The Buddha thereafter wandered through the lower <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Gangetic plain</a>, teaching and building a <a href="/wiki/Sangha" title="Sangha">monastic order</a>. He taught a <a href="/wiki/Middle_Way" title="Middle Way">Middle Way</a> between sensual indulgence and severe asceticism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaumakis20084_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaumakis20084-20">[10]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Bhavana" title="Bhavana">training of the mind</a> that included <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_ethics" title="Buddhist ethics">ethical training</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditative practices</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_effort" title="Right effort">effort</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sati_(Buddhism)" title="Sati (Buddhism)">mindfulness</a>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyāna in Buddhism">jhana</a></i>. He died in <a href="/wiki/Kushinagar" title="Kushinagar">Kushinagar</a>, attaining <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paranirvana" title="Paranirvana">paranirvana</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deathplace_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-deathplace-6">[note 3]</a></sup> The Buddha has since been venerated by numerous religions and communities across <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>.
</p><p>Several centuries after the Buddha's death, his teachings were compiled by the Buddhist community in the <a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">Vinaya</a>, his codes for monastic practice, and the <a href="/wiki/Sutta_Pi%E1%B9%ADaka" title="Sutta Piṭaka">Suttas</a>, texts based on his discourses. These were passed down in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Middle Indo-Aryan languages">Middle Indo-Aryan</a> dialects through an <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199840–41_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199840–41-21">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder20004–7,_44_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder20004–7,_44-22">[12]</a></sup> Later generations composed additional texts, such as systematic treatises known as <i><a href="/wiki/Abhidharma" title="Abhidharma">Abhidharma</a></i>, biographies of the Buddha, collections of stories about his past lives known as <i><a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales">Jataka tales</a></i>, and additional discourses, i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana_sutras" title="Mahayana sutras">Mahayana sutras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder20004_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder20004-23">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox20031–7_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox20031–7-24">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Biography"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Biography</span></a>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_and_titles">Names and titles</span></h2>
<p>Besides "Buddha" and the name Siddhārtha Gautama (Pali: Siddhattha Gotama), he was also known by other names and titles, such as Shakyamuni ("Sage of the <a href="/wiki/Shakya" title="Shakya">Shakyas</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaroni2002230_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaroni2002230-25">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[note 11]</a></sup> The clan name of Gautama means "descendant of Gotama", "Gotama" meaning "one who has the most light",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[16]</a></sup> and comes from the fact that <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriya</a> clans adopted the names of their house priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_(Niche_V1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3244" data-file-width="2197" decoding="async" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_%28Niche_V1%29.jpg/220px-Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_%28Niche_V1%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_%28Niche_V1%29.jpg/330px-Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_%28Niche_V1%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_%28Niche_V1%29.jpg/440px-Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_%28Niche_V1%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tapa_Shotor_seated_Buddha_(Niche_V1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seated Buddha from <a href="/wiki/Tapa_Shotor" title="Tapa Shotor">Tapa Shotor</a> monastery in <a href="/wiki/Hadda,_Afghanistan" title="Hadda, Afghanistan">Hadda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, 2nd century CE</div></div></div>
<p>A common list of <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a> are commonly seen together in the canonical texts, and depict some of his spiritual qualities:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Sammasambuddho</i> – <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood#Samyaksambuddha" title="Buddhahood">Perfectly self-awakened</a></li>
<li><i>Vijja-carana-sampano</i> – Endowed with higher knowledge and ideal conduct.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sugata" title="Sugata">Sugata</a></i> – Well-gone or Well-spoken.</li>
<li><i>Lokavidu</i> – Knower of the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_cosmology" title="Buddhist cosmology">many worlds</a>.</li>
<li><i>Anuttaro Purisa-damma-sarathi</i> – Unexcelled trainer of untrained people.</li>
<li><i>Satthadeva-Manussanam</i> – Teacher of <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Buddhism)" title="Deva (Buddhism)">gods</a> and humans.</li>
<li><i>Bhagavato</i> – <a href="/wiki/Bhagavan" title="Bhagavan">The Blessed one</a></li>
<li><i>Araham</i> – Worthy of homage. An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arahant" title="Arahant">Arahant</a> is "one with taints destroyed, who has lived the holy life, done what had to be done, laid down the burden, reached the true goal, destroyed the fetters of being, and is completely liberated through final knowledge."</li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jina_(Jainism)" title="Jina (Jainism)">Jina</a></i> – Conqueror. Although the term is more commonly used to name an individual who has attained liberation in the religion <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, it is also an alternative title for the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[20]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a> also contains numerous other titles and epithets for the Buddha, including: All-seeing, All-transcending sage, Bull among men, The Caravan leader, Dispeller of darkness, The Eye, Foremost of charioteers, Foremost of those who can cross, King of the Dharma (<i>Dharmaraja</i>), Kinsman of the Sun, Helper of the World (<i>Lokanatha</i>), Lion (<i>Siha</i>), Lord of the Dhamma, Of excellent wisdom (<i>Varapañña</i>), Radiant One, Torchbearer of mankind, Unsurpassed doctor and surgeon, Victor in battle, and Wielder of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_person">Historical person</span></h2>
<p>Scholars are hesitant to make unqualified claims about the historical facts of the Buddha's life. Most of them accept that the Buddha lived, taught, and founded a monastic order during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahajanapada" title="Mahajanapada">Mahajanapada</a> era and during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Bimbisara" title="Bimbisara">Bimbisara</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 558</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 491 BCE</span>, or c. 400 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[24]</a></sup> the ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> empire, and died during the early years of the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ajatashatru" title="Ajatashatru">Ajatashatru</a>, who was the successor of Bimbisara, thus making him a younger contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a>, the Jain <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">tirthankara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith192434,_48_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith192434,_48-36">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann20031–5_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann20031–5-37">[26]</a></sup> While the general sequence of "birth, maturity, renunciation, search, awakening and liberation, teaching, death" is widely accepted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrithers20013_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrithers20013-38">[27]</a></sup> there is less consensus on the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">veracity</a> of many details contained in traditional biographies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuswell2003352_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuswell2003352-39">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELopez199516_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELopez199516-40">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dates of Gautama's birth and death are uncertain. Most historians in the early 20th century dated his lifetime as <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 563 BCE</span> to 483 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECousins199657–63_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECousins199657–63-3">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200310–13_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200310–13-42">[31]</a></sup> Within the Eastern Buddhist tradition of China, Vietnam, Korea and Japan, the traditional date for the death of the Buddha was 949 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECousins199657–63_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECousins199657–63-3">[1]</a></sup> According to the Ka-tan system of time calculation in the <a href="/wiki/Kalachakra" title="Kalachakra">Kalachakra</a> tradition, Buddha is believed to have died about 833 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[32]</a></sup> More recently his death is dated later, between 411 and 400 BCE, while at a symposium on this question held in 1988,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechert_1991–1997[[Category:All_articles_with_incomplete_citations]][[Category:Articles_with_incomplete_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include|&lt;span_title="A_complete_citation_is_needed.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;full_citation_needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_44-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechert_1991–1997[[Category:All_articles_with_incomplete_citations]][[Category:Articles_with_incomplete_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include|&lt;span_title="A_complete_citation_is_needed.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;full_citation_needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-44'>[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuegg199982–87_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuegg199982–87-45">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENarain1993187–201_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENarain1993187–201-46">[35]</a></sup> the majority of those who presented definite opinions gave dates within 20 years either side of 400 BCE for the Buddha's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECousins199657–63_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECousins199657–63-3">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrebish20082_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrebish20082-47">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dating_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-dating-14">[note 8]</a></sup> These alternative chronologies, however, have not been accepted by all historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003xv_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003xv-52">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWayman199737–58_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWayman199737–58-53">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[note 12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_context">Historical context</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mahajanapadas_(c._500_BCE).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Mahajanapadas_%28c._500_BCE%29.png/300px-Mahajanapadas_%28c._500_BCE%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Mahajanapadas_%28c._500_BCE%29.png/450px-Mahajanapadas_%28c._500_BCE%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Mahajanapadas_%28c._500_BCE%29.png/600px-Mahajanapadas_%28c._500_BCE%29.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mahajanapadas_(c._500_BCE).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient kingdoms and cities of India during the time of the Buddha (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">  500 BCE</span>)</div></div></div>
<p>According to the Buddhist tradition, Gautama was born in <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a>, now in modern-day Nepal, and raised in Kapilavastu, which may have been either in what is present-day <a href="/wiki/Tilaurakot" title="Tilaurakot">Tilaurakot</a>, Nepal or <a href="/wiki/Piprahwa" title="Piprahwa">Piprahwa</a>, India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthplace_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-2">[note 2]</a></sup> Indeed, according to the Buddhist tradition, following the <i>Nidanakatha</i>,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausböllDavidsDavids1878[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_59-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausböllDavidsDavids1878[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_March_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(March_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-59'>[47]</a></sup> the introductory to the <a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales">Jataka tales</a>, the stories of the former lives of the Buddha, Gautama was born in Lumbini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHC_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-60">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Victoria_and_Albert_Museum_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Victoria_and_Albert_Museum-61">[49]</a></sup> In the mid-3rd century BCE the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a> determined that Lumbini was Gautama's birthplace and thus installed a pillar there with the inscription: "...this is where the Buddha, sage of the Śākyas (<i>Śākyamuni</i>), was born."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199819_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199819-62">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on stone inscriptions, there is also speculation that Lumbei, Kapileswar village, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, at the east coast of India, was the site of ancient Lumbini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahāpātra1977_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahāpātra1977-63">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMohāpātra2000114_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMohāpātra2000114-64">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathy2014_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathy2014-65">[53]</a></sup> Hartmann discusses the hypothesis and states, "The inscription has generally been considered spurious (...)"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartmann199138–39_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartmann199138–39-66">[54]</a></sup> He quotes Sircar: "There can hardly be any doubt that the people responsible for the Kapilesvara inscription copied it from the said facsimile not much earlier than 1928." Kapilavastu was the place where he grew up:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436-67">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[note 13]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Warder: "The Buddha [...] was born in the Sakya Republic, which was the city state of Kapilavastu, a very small state just inside the modern state boundary of Nepal against the Northern Indian frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder200045_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder200045-13">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li>Walshe: "He belonged to the Sakya clan dwelling on the edge of the Himalayas, his actual birthplace being a few kilometres north of the present-day Northern Indian border, in Nepal. His father was, in fact, an elected chief of the clan rather than the king he was later made out to be, though his title was <i>raja</i>—a term which only partly corresponds to our word 'king'. Some of the states of North India at that time were kingdoms and others republics, and the Sakyan republic was subject to the powerful king of neighbouring Kosala, which lay to the south".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalshe199520_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalshe199520-70">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>The exact location of ancient Kapilavastu is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436-67">[55]</a></sup> It may have been either <a href="/wiki/Piprahwa" title="Piprahwa">Piprahwa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, northern India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura198018_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura198018-71">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESrivastava197961–74_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESrivastava197961–74-72">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESrivastava1980108_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESrivastava1980108-73">[60]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Tilaurakot" title="Tilaurakot">Tilaurakot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETuladhar20021–7_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETuladhar20021–7-74">[61]</a></sup> present-day Nepal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986-75">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436-67">[55]</a></sup> The two cities are located only 24 kilometres (15 miles) from each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986-75">[62]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>See also <a href="#Birth_and_early_life">Conception and birth</a>. According to Buddhist tradition, he obtained his enlightenment in <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>, gave his first sermon in <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Sarnath</a>, and died in <a href="/wiki/Kushinagar" title="Kushinagar">Kushinagar</a>.
</p><p>One of Gautama's usual names was "Sakamuni" or "Sakyamunī" ("Sage of the Shakyas"). This and the evidence of the early texts suggests that he was born into the Shakya clan, a community that was on the periphery, both geographically and culturally, of the eastern Indian subcontinent in the 5th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich198849_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich198849-76">[63]</a></sup> The community, though describable as a small republic, was probably an <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>, with his father as the elected chieftain or oligarch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich198849_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich198849-76">[63]</a></sup> The Shakyas were widely considered to be non-Vedic (and, hence impure) in Brahminic texts; their origins remain speculative and debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levman2013_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levman2013-77">[64]</a></sup> Bronkhorst terms this culture, which grew in the peripheries of Aryavarta without being affected by the flourish of Brahminism, as <a href="/wiki/Greater_Magadha" title="Greater Magadha">Greater Magadha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedic</a> <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmins</a>, the Buddha's lifetime coincided with the flourishing of influential <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Śramaṇa">śramaṇa</a> schools of thought like <a href="/wiki/%C4%80j%C4%ABvika" title="Ājīvika">Ājīvika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Cārvāka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aj%C3%B1ana" title="Ajñana">Ajñana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963chpt._1–3_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963chpt._1–3-79">[66]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmajala_Sutta" title="Brahmajala Sutta">Brahmajala Sutta</a></i> records sixty-two such schools of thought. In this context, a śramaṇa refers to one who labours, toils or exerts themselves (for some higher or religious purpose). It was also the age of influential thinkers like <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Purana_Kassapa" title="Purana Kassapa">Pūraṇa Kassapa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makkhali_Gosala" title="Makkhali Gosala">Makkhali Gosāla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajita_Kesakambali" title="Ajita Kesakambali">Ajita Kesakambalī</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakudha_Kaccayana" title="Pakudha Kaccayana">Pakudha Kaccāyana</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanjaya_Belatthaputta" title="Sanjaya Belatthaputta">Sañjaya Belaṭṭhaputta</a>, as recorded in <a href="/wiki/Sama%C3%B1%C3%B1aphala_Sutta" title="Samaññaphala Sutta">Samaññaphala Sutta</a>, with whose viewpoints the Buddha must have been acquainted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalshe1995268_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalshe1995268-81">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2009199–200_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2009199–200-82">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[note 14]</a></sup> Indeed, <a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C4%81riputra" title="Śāriputra">Śāriputra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maudgalyayana" title="Maudgalyayana">Moggallāna</a>, two of the foremost disciples of the Buddha, were formerly the foremost disciples of Sañjaya Belaṭṭhaputta, the sceptic;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura198020_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura198020-85">[71]</a></sup> and the Pāli canon frequently depicts Buddha engaging in debate with the adherents of rival schools of thought. There is also philological evidence to suggest that the two masters, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alara_Kalama" title="Alara Kalama">Alara Kalama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uddaka_R%C4%81maputta" title="Uddaka Rāmaputta">Uddaka Rāmaputta</a>, were indeed historical figures and they most probably taught Buddha two different forms of meditative techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne20078–23ch._2_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne20078–23ch._2-86">[72]</a></sup> Thus, Buddha was just one of the many śramaṇa philosophers of that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder199845_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder199845-87">[73]</a></sup> In an era where holiness of person was judged by their level of asceticism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoy19841_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoy19841-88">[74]</a></sup> Buddha was a reformist within the śramaṇa movement, rather than a reactionary against Vedic Brahminism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoy19847_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoy19847-89">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, the life of the Buddha also coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_conquest_of_the_Indus_Valley" title="Achaemenid conquest of the Indus Valley">Achaemenid conquest of the Indus Valley</a> during the rule of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> from about 517/516 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIB-90">[76]</a></sup> This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenid</a> occupation of the areas of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, which lasted about two centuries, was accompanied by the introduction of Achaemenid religions, reformed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazdaism" title="Mazdaism">Mazdaism</a> or early <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, to which Buddhism might have in part reacted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIB-90">[76]</a></sup> In particular, the ideas of the Buddha may have partly consisted of a rejection of the "absolutist" or "perfectionist" ideas contained in these Achaemenid religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIB_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIB-90">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_sources">Earliest sources</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_Texts" title="Early Buddhist Texts">Early Buddhist Texts</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:192px;max-width:192px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:190px;max-width:190px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:180px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lumbini_inscription_(complete).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3094" data-file-width="3217" decoding="async" height="181" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lumbini_inscription_%28complete%29.jpg/376px-Lumbini_inscription_%28complete%29.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:190px;max-width:190px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:57px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_Sakyamuni_on_the_Rummindei_pillar_of_Ashoka.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="318" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="57" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Buddha_Sakyamuni_on_the_Rummindei_pillar_of_Ashoka.jpg/188px-Buddha_Sakyamuni_on_the_Rummindei_pillar_of_Ashoka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Buddha_Sakyamuni_on_the_Rummindei_pillar_of_Ashoka.jpg/282px-Buddha_Sakyamuni_on_the_Rummindei_pillar_of_Ashoka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Buddha_Sakyamuni_on_the_Rummindei_pillar_of_Ashoka.jpg/376px-Buddha_Sakyamuni_on_the_Rummindei_pillar_of_Ashoka.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Lumbini_pillar_inscription" title="Lumbini pillar inscription">Lumbini pillar inscription</a> (c. 250 BCE), with the words "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Bu-dhe</a>" (𑀩𑀼𑀥𑁂, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>) and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakyas" title="Shakyas">Sa-kya</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muni_(Saint)" title="Muni (Saint)">mu-nī</a> " (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%91%80%B2%F0%91%80%93%F0%91%81%86%F0%91%80%AC%F0%91%80%AB%F0%91%80%BC%F0%91%80%A6%F0%91%80%BB" title="wikt:𑀲𑀓𑁆𑀬𑀫𑀼𑀦𑀻">𑀲𑀓𑁆𑀬𑀫𑀼𑀦𑀻</a>, "Sage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakyas" title="Shakyas">Shakyas</a>") in the <a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brahmi script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeise201346–47_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeise201346–47-91">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brill_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brill-92">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>No written records about Gautama were found from his lifetime or from the one or two centuries thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WTB8_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-WTB8-94">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LF72_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-LF72-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[82]</a></sup> But from the middle of the 3rd century BCE, several <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a> (reigned c. 269–232 BCE) mention the Buddha and Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WTB8_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-WTB8-94">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LF72_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-LF72-95">[81]</a></sup> Particularly, <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Lumbini_pillar_inscription" title="Lumbini pillar inscription">Lumbini pillar inscription</a> commemorates the Emperor's pilgrimage to Lumbini as the Buddha's birthplace, calling him the <i>Buddha Shakyamuni</i> (<a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brahmi script</a>: 𑀩𑀼𑀥 <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%91%80%B2%F0%91%80%93%F0%91%81%86%F0%91%80%AC%F0%91%80%AB%F0%91%80%BC%F0%91%80%A6%F0%91%80%BB" title="wikt:𑀲𑀓𑁆𑀬𑀫𑀼𑀦𑀻">𑀲𑀓𑁆𑀬𑀫𑀼𑀦𑀻</a> <i>Bu-dha Sa-kya-mu-nī</i>, "Buddha, Sage of the Shakyas").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive.org_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive.org-97">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeise201346–47_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeise201346–47-91">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brill_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brill-92">[78]</a></sup> Another one of his edicts (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_Rock_Edict" title="Minor Rock Edict">Minor Rock Edict No. 3</a>) mentions the titles of several <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dhamma</a></i> texts (in Buddhism, "dhamma" is another word for "dharma"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[84]</a></sup> establishing the existence of a written Buddhist tradition at least by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya era</a>. These texts may be the precursor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li_Canon" title="Pāli Canon">Pāli Canon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhammika1993_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhammika1993-99">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[note 15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="877" data-file-width="2414" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg/300px-Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg/450px-Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg/600px-Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bhagavato_Sakamunino_Bodho_inscription_in_Bharhut.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inscription "The illumination of the Blessed Sakamuni" (<a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brahmi script</a>: 𑀪𑀕𑀯𑀢𑁄 𑀲𑀓𑀫𑀼𑀦𑀺𑀦𑁄 𑀩𑁄𑀥𑁄, <i>Bhagavato Sakamunino Bodho</i>) on a relief showing the "<a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Buddhism" title="Aniconism in Buddhism">empty</a>" <i><a href="/wiki/Vajrasana,_Bodh_Gaya" title="Vajrasana, Bodh Gaya">Illumination Throne</a></i> of the Buddha in the early <a href="/wiki/Mahabodhi_Temple" title="Mahabodhi Temple">Mahabodhi Temple</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bharhut" title="Bharhut">Bharhut</a>, c. 100 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JL_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-JL-102">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KTSS80_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-KTSS80-103">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[89]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>"Sakamuni" is also mentioned in the reliefs of <a href="/wiki/Bharhut" title="Bharhut">Bharhut</a>, dated to c. 100 BCE, in relation with his illumination and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bodhi_tree" title="Bodhi tree">Bodhi tree</a>, with the inscription <i>Bhagavato Sakamunino Bodho</i> ("The illumination of the Blessed Sakamuni").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KTSS80_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-KTSS80-103">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JL_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-JL-102">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest surviving Buddhist manuscripts are the <a href="/wiki/Gandh%C4%81ran_Buddhist_texts" title="Gandhāran Buddhist texts">Gandhāran Buddhist texts</a>, found in <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> (corresponding to modern northwestern Pakistan and eastern Afghanistan) and written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C4%81ndh%C4%81r%C4%AB_language" title="Gāndhārī language">Gāndhārī</a>, they date from the first century BCE to the third century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the basis of <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philological</a> evidence, Indologist and Pāli expert <a href="/wiki/Oskar_von_Hin%C3%BCber" title="Oskar von Hinüber">Oskar von Hinüber</a> says that some of the Pāli suttas have retained very archaic place-names, syntax, and historical data from close to the Buddha's lifetime, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81parinibb%C4%81%E1%B9%87a_Sutta" title="Mahāparinibbāṇa Sutta">Mahāparinibbāṇa Sutta</a></i> which contains a detailed account of the Buddha's final days. Hinüber proposes a composition date of no later than 350–320 BCE for this text, which would allow for a "true historical memory" of the events approximately 60 years prior if the Short Chronology for the Buddha's lifetime is accepted (but he also points out that such a text was originally intended more as <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a> than as an exact historical record of events).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Hinüber2008198–206_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Hinüber2008198–206-106">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>John S. Strong sees certain biographical fragments in the canonical texts preserved in Pāli, as well as Chinese, Tibetan and Sanskrit as the earliest material. These include texts such as the "Discourse on the Noble Quest" (: <i>Ariyapariyesanā-sutta</i>) and its parallels in other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong20015_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong20015-108">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_biographies">Traditional biographies</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BimaranCasket2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2618" data-file-width="2123" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/BimaranCasket2.JPG/220px-BimaranCasket2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/BimaranCasket2.JPG/330px-BimaranCasket2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/BimaranCasket2.JPG/440px-BimaranCasket2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BimaranCasket2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the earliest anthropomorphic representations of the Buddha, here surrounded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_(Buddhism)" title="Brahma (Buddhism)">Brahma</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aakra_(Buddhism)" title="Śakra (Buddhism)">Śakra</a> (right). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bimaran_Casket" title="Bimaran Casket">Bimaran Casket</a>, mid-1st century CE, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[95]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biographical_sources">Biographical sources</span></h3>
<p>The sources which present a complete picture of the life of Siddhārtha Gautama are a variety of different, and sometimes conflicting, traditional biographies. These include the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhacarita" title="Buddhacarita">Buddhacarita</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lalitavistara_S%C5%ABtra" title="Lalitavistara Sūtra">Lalitavistara Sūtra</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81vastu" title="Mahāvastu">Mahāvastu</a></i>, and the <i>Nidānakathā</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler200532_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler200532-111">[96]</a></sup> Of these, the <i>Buddhacarita</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeal1883_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeal1883-112">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowell1894_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowell1894-113">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillemen2009_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillemen2009-114">[99]</a></sup> is the earliest full biography, an epic poem written by the poet <a href="/wiki/A%C5%9Bvagho%E1%B9%A3a" title="Aśvaghoṣa">Aśvaghoṣa</a> in the first century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[100]</a></sup> The <i>Lalitavistara Sūtra</i> is the next oldest biography, a <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahāyāna</a>/<a href="/wiki/Sarvastivada" title="Sarvastivada">Sarvāstivāda</a> biography dating to the 3rd century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaretzky2000xxi_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaretzky2000xxi-116">[101]</a></sup> The <i>Mahāvastu</i> from the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81s%C4%81%E1%B9%83ghika" title="Mahāsāṃghika">Mahāsāṃghika</a> <a href="/wiki/Lokottarav%C4%81da" title="Lokottaravāda">Lokottaravāda</a> tradition is another major biography, composed incrementally until perhaps the 4th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaretzky2000xxi_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaretzky2000xxi-116">[101]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a> biography of the Buddha is the most exhaustive, and is entitled the <i>Abhiniṣkramaṇa Sūtra</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeal1875_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeal1875-117">[102]</a></sup> and various Chinese translations of this date between the 3rd and 6th century CE. The <i>Nidānakathā</i> is from the <a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada">Theravada</a> tradition in Sri Lanka and was composed in the 5th century by <a href="/wiki/Buddhaghosa" title="Buddhaghosa">Buddhaghoṣa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwearer2004177_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwearer2004177-118">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earlier canonical sources include the <i>Ariyapariyesana Sutta</i> (<a href="/wiki/Majjhima_Nik%C4%81ya" title="Majjhima Nikāya">MN</a> 26), the <i>Mahāparinibbāṇa Sutta</i> (<a href="/wiki/D%C4%ABgha_Nik%C4%81ya" title="Dīgha Nikāya">DN</a> 16), the <i>Mahāsaccaka-sutta</i> (MN 36), the <i>Mahapadana Sutta</i> (DN 14), and the <i>Achariyabhuta Sutta</i> (MN 123), which include selective accounts that may be older, but are not full biographies. The <a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales">Jātaka tales</a> retell previous lives of Gautama as a <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisattva</a>, and the first collection of these can be dated among the earliest Buddhist texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchober200220_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchober200220-119">[104]</a></sup> The <i>Mahāpadāna Sutta</i> and <i>Achariyabhuta Sutta</i> both recount miraculous events surrounding Gautama's birth, such as the bodhisattva's descent from the <a href="/wiki/Tushita" title="Tushita">Tuṣita Heaven</a> into his mother's womb.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_traditional_depictions">Nature of traditional depictions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="453" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg/220px-Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg/330px-Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg/440px-Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astasahasrika_Prajnaparamita_Queen_Maya_Birth.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Maya_(mother_of_the_Buddha)" title="Maya (mother of the Buddha)">Māyā</a> miraculously giving birth to Siddhārtha. <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palm-leaf_manuscript" title="Palm-leaf manuscript">palm-leaf manuscript</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nālandā</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, India. <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Pāla period</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the earliest Buddhist texts, the <a href="/wiki/Nik%C4%81ya" title="Nikāya">nikāyas</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80gama_(Buddhism)" title="Āgama (Buddhism)">āgamas</a>, the Buddha is not depicted as possessing <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a> (<i>sabbaññu</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2006_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2006-120">[105]</a></sup> nor is he depicted as being an eternal transcendent (<i>lokottara</i>) being. According to <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Analayo" title="Bhikkhu Analayo">Bhikkhu Analayo</a>, ideas of the Buddha's omniscience (along with an increasing tendency to deify him and his biography) are found only later, in the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana_sutras" title="Mahayana sutras">Mahayana sutras</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a> commentaries or texts such as the <i>Mahāvastu</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2006_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2006-120">[105]</a></sup> In the <i>Sandaka Sutta</i>, the Buddha's disciple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ananda" title="Ananda">Ananda</a> outlines an argument against the claims of teachers who say they are all knowing <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[106]</a></sup> while in the <i>Tevijjavacchagotta Sutta</i> the Buddha himself states that he has never made a claim to being omniscient, instead he claimed to have the "higher knowledges" (<a href="/wiki/Abhij%C3%B1%C4%81" title="Abhijñā">abhijñā</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[107]</a></sup> The earliest biographical material from the Pali Nikayas focuses on the Buddha's life as a śramaṇa, his search for enlightenment under various teachers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alara_Kalama" title="Alara Kalama">Alara Kalama</a> and his forty-five-year career as a teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional biographies of Gautama often include numerous miracles, omens, and supernatural events. The character of the Buddha in these traditional biographies is often that of a fully transcendent (Skt. <i>lokottara</i>) and perfected being who is unencumbered by the mundane world. In the <i>Mahāvastu</i>, over the course of many lives, Gautama is said to have developed supramundane abilities including: a painless birth conceived without intercourse; no need for sleep, food, medicine, or bathing, although engaging in such "in conformity with the world"; omniscience, and the ability to "suppress karma".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1956[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_124-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1956[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-124'>[109]</a></sup> As noted by Andrew Skilton, the Buddha was often described as being superhuman, including descriptions of him having the 32 major and 80 minor marks of a "great man", and the idea that the Buddha could live for as long as an aeon if he wished (see DN 16).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkilton200464–65_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkilton200464–65-125">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancient Indians were generally unconcerned with chronologies, being more focused on philosophy. Buddhist texts reflect this tendency, providing a clearer picture of what Gautama may have taught than of the dates of the events in his life. These texts contain descriptions of the culture and daily life of ancient India which can be corroborated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain_Agamas_(%C5%9Av%C4%93t%C4%81mbara)" title="Jain Agamas (Śvētāmbara)">Jain scriptures</a>, and make the Buddha's time the earliest period in <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">Indian history</a> for which significant accounts exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrithers200115_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrithers200115-126">[111]</a></sup> British author <a href="/wiki/Karen_Armstrong" title="Karen Armstrong">Karen Armstrong</a> writes that although there is very little information that can be considered historically sound, we can be reasonably confident that Siddhārtha Gautama did exist as a historical figure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong2000xii_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong2000xii-127">[112]</a></sup> Michael Carrithers goes a bit further by stating that the most general outline of "birth, maturity, renunciation, search, awakening and liberation, teaching, death" must be true.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrithers2001[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_May_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(May_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_128-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrithers2001[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_May_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(May_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-128'>[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Previous_lives">Previous lives</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg/220px-Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg/330px-Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg/440px-Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ascetic_Sumedha_and_Dipankara_Buddha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The legendary Jataka collections depict the Buddha-to-be in a previous life prostrating before the past Buddha <a href="/wiki/Dipankara" title="Dipankara">Dipankara</a>, making a resolve to be a Buddha, and receiving a prediction of future Buddhahood.</div></div></div>
<p>Legendary biographies like the Pali <i><a href="/wiki/Buddhava%E1%B9%83sa" title="Buddhavaṃsa">Buddhavaṃsa</a></i> and the Sanskrit <i>Jātakamālā</i> depict the Buddha's (referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">bodhisattva</a>" before his awakening) career as spanning hundreds of lifetimes before his last birth as Gautama. Many stories of these previous lives are depicted in the <a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales">Jatakas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200119_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200119-129">[114]</a></sup> The format of a Jataka typically begins by telling a story in the present which is then explained by a story of someone's previous life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200121_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200121-130">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides imbuing the pre-Buddhist past with a deep karmic history, the Jatakas also serve to explain the bodhisattva's (the Buddha-to-be) path to Buddhahood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200124_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200124-131">[116]</a></sup> In biographies like the <i>Buddhavaṃsa</i>, this path is described as long and arduous, taking "four incalculable ages" (<i><a href="/wiki/Asa%E1%B9%83khyeya" title="Asaṃkhyeya">asamkheyyas</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200130_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200130-132">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>In these legendary biographies, the bodhisattva goes through many different births (animal and human), is inspired by his meeting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_the_named_Buddhas" title="List of the named Buddhas">past Buddhas</a>, and then makes a series of resolves or vows (<i><a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva_vow" title="Bodhisattva vow">pranidhana</a></i>) to become a Buddha himself. Then he begins to receive predictions by past Buddhas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200131_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200131-133">[118]</a></sup> One of the most popular of these stories is <a href="/wiki/Sumedha" title="Sumedha">his meeting with Dipankara Buddha</a>, who gives the bodhisattva a prediction of future Buddhahood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200125_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200125-134">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another theme found in the Pali Jataka Commentary (<i>Jātakaṭṭhakathā</i>) and the Sanskrit <i>Jātakamālā</i> is how the Buddha-to-be had to practice several "perfections" (<i><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81ramit%C4%81" title="Pāramitā">pāramitā</a></i>) to reach Buddhahood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200137_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200137-135">[120]</a></sup> The Jatakas also sometimes depict negative actions done in previous lives by the bodhisattva, which explain difficulties he experienced in his final life as Gautama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200143_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200143-136">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_early_life">Birth and early life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg/300px-Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg/450px-Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg/600px-Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_pilgrimage_sites_in_India.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a> and other major Buddhist sites in India. <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a> (present-day Nepal), is the birthplace of the Buddha,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHC_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-60">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthplace_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-2">[note 2]</a></sup> and is a holy place also for many non-Buddhists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google260_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-google260-137">[122]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4159" data-file-width="3462" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg/220px-Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg/330px-Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg/440px-Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lumbini_pillar_with_inscription_and_its_location.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Lumbini_pillar_inscription" title="Lumbini pillar inscription">Lumbini pillar contains an inscription</a> stating that this is the Buddha's birthplace</div></div></div>
<p>The Buddhist tradition regards <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a>, in present-day Nepal, and <a href="/wiki/Kapilavastu_(ancient_city)" title="Kapilavastu (ancient city)">Kapilavastu</a>, as the Buddha's birthplace and childhood home, respectively.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeise2013[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_138-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeise2013[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-138'>[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthplace_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-2">[note 2]</a></sup> The exact site of ancient Kapilavastu is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrainor2010436–437_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrainor2010436–437-139">[124]</a></sup> It may have been either <a href="/wiki/Piprahwa" title="Piprahwa">Piprahwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, in present-day India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura198018_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura198018-71">[58]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Tilaurakot" title="Tilaurakot">Tilaurakot</a>, in present-day Nepal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986-75">[62]</a></sup> Both places belonged to the Sakya territory, and are located only 24 kilometres (15 mi) apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986-75">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to later biographies such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81vastu" title="Mahāvastu">Mahavastu</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Lalitavistara_S%C5%ABtra" title="Lalitavistara Sūtra">Lalitavistara</a></i>, his mother, <a href="/wiki/Maya_(mother_of_the_Buddha)" title="Maya (mother of the Buddha)">Maya</a> (Māyādevī), Suddhodana's wife, was a <a href="/wiki/Koliya" title="Koliya">Koliyan</a> princess. Legend has it that, on the night Siddhartha was conceived, Queen Maya dreamt that a <a href="/wiki/White_elephant_(animal)" title="White elephant (animal)">white elephant</a> with six white tusks entered her right side,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeal187537_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeal187537-140">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones195211_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones195211-141">[126]</a></sup> and ten <a href="/wiki/Lunar_month" title="Lunar month">months</a> later<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeal187541_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeal187541-142">[127]</a></sup> Siddhartha was born. As was the Shakya tradition, when his mother Queen Maya became pregnant, she left Kapilavastu for her father's kingdom to give birth. However, her son is said to have been born on the way, at Lumbini, in a garden beneath a <a href="/wiki/Shorea_robusta" title="Shorea robusta">sal tree</a>. The earliest Buddhist sources state that the Buddha was born to an aristocratic <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriya</a> (Pali: <i>khattiya</i>) family called Gotama (Sanskrit: Gautama), who were part of the <a href="/wiki/Shakya" title="Shakya">Shakyas</a>, a tribe of rice-farmers living near the modern border of India and Nepal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199021_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199021-143">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199814_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199814-68">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel2010_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel2010-144">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[note 16]</a></sup> His father <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Auddhodana" title="Śuddhodana">Śuddhodana</a> was "an elected chief of the <a href="/wiki/Shakya" title="Shakya">Shakya clan</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder200045_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder200045-13">[6]</a></sup> whose capital was Kapilavastu, and who were later annexed by the growing Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Kosala" title="Kosala">Kosala</a> during the Buddha's lifetime. Gautama was his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_name" title="Family name">family name</a>.
</p><p>The early Buddhist texts contain very little information about the birth and youth of Gotama Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu19928_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu19928-147">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200151_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200151-148">[132]</a></sup> Later biographies developed a dramatic narrative about the life of the young Gotama as a prince and his existential troubles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199024_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199024-149">[133]</a></sup> They also depict his father Śuddhodana as a <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_monarchy" title="Hereditary monarchy">hereditary monarch</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suryavansha" title="Suryavansha">Suryavansha</a> (Solar dynasty) of <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Ikṣvāku</i></span> (Pāli: Okkāka). This is unlikely however, as many scholars think that Śuddhodana was merely a Shakya aristocrat (<i>khattiya</i>), and that the Shakya republic was not a hereditary monarchy.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhammikan.d.[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_150-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhammikan.d.[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-150'>[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199814–15_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199814–15-151">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich198849–50_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich198849–50-152">[136]</a></sup> Indeed, the more egalitarian <a href="/wiki/Ga%E1%B9%87asa%E1%B9%85gha" title="Gaṇasaṅgha"><span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn">gaṇasaṅgha</i></span></a> form of government, as a political alternative to Indian monarchies, may have influenced the development of the śramanic <a href="/wiki/Jain_monasticism" title="Jain monasticism">Jain</a> and Buddhist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sangha_(Buddhism)" title="Sangha (Buddhism)">sanghas</a>, where monarchies tended toward <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Vedic Brahmanism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThapar2002146_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2002146-153">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>The day of the Buddha's birth is widely celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada">Theravada</a> countries as <a href="/wiki/Vesak" title="Vesak">Vesak</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurpie20013_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurpie20013-154">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buddha%27s_Birthday" title="Buddha's Birthday">Buddha's Birthday</a> is called <i>Buddha <a href="/wiki/Purnima" title="Purnima">Purnima</a></i> in Nepal, Bangladesh, and India as he is believed to have been born on a full moon day.
</p><p>According to later biographical legends, during the birth celebrations, the hermit seer <a href="/wiki/Asita" title="Asita">Asita</a> journeyed from his mountain abode, analyzed the child for the <a href="/wiki/Physical_characteristics_of_the_Buddha" title="Physical characteristics of the Buddha">"32 marks of a great man"</a> and then announced that he would either become a great king (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chakravartin" title="Chakravartin">chakravartin</a></i>) or a great religious leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENarada19929–12_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENarada19929–12-155">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200155_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200155-156">[140]</a></sup> Suddhodana held a naming ceremony on the fifth day and invited eight <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmin</a> scholars to read the future. All gave similar predictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENarada19929–12_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENarada19929–12-155">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kaundinya" title="Kaundinya">Kondañña</a>, the youngest, and later to be the first <a href="/wiki/Arhat" title="Arhat">arhat</a> other than the Buddha, was reputed to be the only one who unequivocally predicted that Siddhartha would become a <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood" title="Buddhahood">Buddha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENarada199211–12_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENarada199211–12-157">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early texts suggest that Gautama was not familiar with the dominant religious teachings of his time until he left on his religious quest, which is said to have been motivated by existential concern for the human condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton200047_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton200047-158">[142]</a></sup> According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_Texts" title="Early Buddhist Texts">early Buddhist Texts</a> of several schools, and numerous <a href="/wiki/Post-canonical_Buddhist_texts" title="Post-canonical Buddhist texts">post-canonical accounts</a>, Gotama had a wife, <a href="/wiki/Ya%C5%9Bodhar%C4%81" title="Yaśodharā">Yasodhara</a>, and a son, named <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81hula" title="Rāhula">Rāhula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeeks2016139_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeeks2016139-159">[143]</a></sup> Besides this, the Buddha in the early texts reports that "'I lived a spoilt, a very spoilt life, monks (in my parents' home)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200323_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200323-160">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The legendary biographies like the <i><a href="/wiki/Lalitavistara_S%C5%ABtra" title="Lalitavistara Sūtra">Lalitavistara</a></i> also tell stories of young Gotama's great martial skill, which was put to the test in various contests against other Shakyan youths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200160_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200160-161">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renunciation">Renunciation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Great_Renunciation" title="Great Renunciation">Great Renunciation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Departure.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="4154" decoding="async" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Great_Departure.JPG/300px-Great_Departure.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Great_Departure.JPG/450px-Great_Departure.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Great_Departure.JPG/600px-Great_Departure.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Departure.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "Great Departure" of Siddhartha Gautama, surrounded by a <a href="/wiki/Halo_(religious_iconography)" title="Halo (religious iconography)">halo</a>, he is accompanied by numerous guards and <a href="/wiki/Devata" title="Devata">devata</a> who have come to pay homage; <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan period</a></div></div></div>
<p>While the earliest sources merely depict Gotama seeking a higher spiritual goal and becoming an ascetic or <i>śramaṇa</i> after being disillusioned with lay life, the later legendary biographies tell a more elaborate dramatic story about how he became a mendicant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199024_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199024-149">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199815_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199815-162">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest accounts of the Buddha's spiritual quest is found in texts such as the Pali <i>Ariyapariyesanā-sutta</i> ("The discourse on the noble quest," MN 26) and its Chinese parallel at <a href="/wiki/Madhyama_Agama" title="Madhyama Agama">MĀ</a> 204.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011170_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011170-163">[147]</a></sup> These texts report that what led to Gautama's renunciation was the thought that his life was subject to old age, disease and death and that there might be something better (i.e. liberation, nirvana).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-164">[148]</a></sup> The early texts also depict the Buddha's explanation for becoming a sramana as follows: "The household life, this place of impurity, is narrow – the <i>samana</i> life is the free open air. It is not easy for a householder to lead the perfected, utterly pure and perfect holy life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200345_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200345-165">[149]</a></sup> MN 26, MĀ 204, the <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a> Vinaya and the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81vastu" title="Mahāvastu">Mahāvastu</a> all agree that his mother and father opposed his decision and "wept with tearful faces" when he decided to leave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200345–46_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200345–46-166">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011173_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011173-167">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1440" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg/220px-Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg/330px-Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg/440px-Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siddharta_Gautama_Borobudur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prince Siddhartha shaves his hair and becomes a <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Śramaṇa">śramaṇa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">Borobudur</a>, 8th century</div></div></div>
