<p>Legendary biographies also tell the story of how Gautama left his palace to see the outside world for the first time and how he was shocked by his encounter with human suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199821_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199821-168">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200163_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200163-169">[153]</a></sup> These depict Gautama's father as shielding him from religious teachings and from knowledge of human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">suffering</a>, so that he would become a great king instead of a great religious leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199820_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199820-170">[154]</a></sup> In the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nidanakatha" title="Nidanakatha">Nidanakatha</a></i> (5th century CE), Gautama is said to have seen an old man. When his charioteer <a href="/wiki/Channa_(Buddhist)" title="Channa (Buddhist)">Chandaka</a> explained to him that all people grew old, the prince went on further trips beyond the palace. On these he encountered a diseased man, a decaying corpse, and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shramana" title="Shramana">ascetic</a> that inspired him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConze195939–40_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConze195939–40-171">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder2000322_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder2000322-172">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200344_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200344-173">[157]</a></sup> This story of the "<a href="/wiki/Four_sights" title="Four sights">four sights</a>" seems to be adapted from an earlier account in the <i>Digha Nikaya</i> (DN 14.2) which instead depicts the young life of a previous Buddha, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vipass%C4%AB_Buddha" title="Vipassī Buddha">Vipassi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200344_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200344-173">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>The legendary biographies depict Gautama's departure from his palace as follows. Shortly after seeing the four sights, Gautama woke up at night and saw his female servants lying in unattractive, corpse-like poses, which shocked him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001Incitements_to_Leave_Home_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001Incitements_to_Leave_Home-174">[158]</a></sup> Therefore, he discovered what he would later understand more deeply during his <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Buddhism" title="Enlightenment in Buddhism">enlightenment</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">suffering</a> and the end of suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2015The_Beginnings_of_Discontent_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2015The_Beginnings_of_Discontent-175">[159]</a></sup> Moved by all the things he had experienced, he decided to leave the palace in the middle of the night against the will of his father, to live the life of a wandering ascetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConze195939–40_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConze195939–40-171">[155]</a></sup> Accompanied by Chandaka and riding his horse <a href="/wiki/Kanthaka" title="Kanthaka">Kanthaka</a>, Gautama leaves the palace, leaving behind his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rahula" title="Rahula">Rahula</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yashodhara" title="Yashodhara">Yaśodhara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENarada199215–16_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENarada199215–16-176">[160]</a></sup> He traveled to the river <a href="/wiki/Anoma_River" title="Anoma River">Anomiya</a>, and cut off his hair. Leaving his servant and horse behind, he journeyed into the woods and changed into <a href="/wiki/Kasaya_(clothing)" title="Kasaya (clothing)">monk's robes</a> there,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2015The_Great_Departure_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2015The_Great_Departure-177">[161]</a></sup> though in some other versions of the story, he received the robes from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_(Buddhism)" title="Brahma (Buddhism)"><i>Brahma</i></a> deity at Anomiya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPenner200928_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPenner200928-178">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the legendary biographies, when the ascetic Gautama first went to Rajagaha (present-day <a href="/wiki/Rajgir" title="Rajgir">Rajgir</a>) to beg for <a href="/wiki/Alms#Buddhism" title="Alms">alms</a> in the streets, King <a href="/wiki/Bimbisara" title="Bimbisara">Bimbisara</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> learned of his quest, and offered him a share of his kingdom. Gautama rejected the offer but promised to visit his kingdom first, upon attaining enlightenment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001The_Great_Departure_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001The_Great_Departure-179">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199025_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199025-180">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascetic_life_and_awakening">Ascetic life and awakening</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg/220px-Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg/330px-Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg/440px-Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wat_Suthat_%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%B8%E0%B8%97%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%A8%E0%B8%99%E0%B9%8C_-_emaciated_fasting_Buddha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The gilded "Emaciated Buddha statue" in <a href="/wiki/Wat_Suthat" title="Wat Suthat">Wat Suthat</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a> representing the stage of his <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg/220px-Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg/330px-Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg/440px-Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bodhgaya_3639641913_f4c5f73689_t.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Mahabodhi Tree at the Sri <a href="/wiki/Mahabodhi_Temple" title="Mahabodhi Temple">Mahabodhi Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vajrasana,_early_20th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="508" data-file-width="919" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg/220px-Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg/330px-Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg/440px-Vajrasana%2C_early_20th_century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vajrasana,_early_20th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Vajrasana,_Bodh_Gaya" title="Vajrasana, Bodh Gaya">Enlightenment Throne</a></i> of the Buddha at <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>, as recreated by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a> in the 3rd century BCE.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3429" data-file-width="2525" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg/220px-Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg/330px-Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg/440px-Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miracle_of_the_Buddha_walking_on_a_River_-_East_Face_-_South_Pillar_-_East_Gateway_-_Stupa_1_-_Sanchi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miracle of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> walking on the River Nairañjanā. The Buddha is not visible (<a href="/wiki/Aniconism" title="Aniconism">aniconism</a>), only represented by a path on the water, and his empty throne bottom right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall191865_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall191865-181">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Buddhism" title="Enlightenment in Buddhism">Enlightenment in Buddhism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">Moksha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">Nirvana (Buddhism)</a></div>
<p>Majjhima Nikaya 4 mentions that Gautama lived in "remote jungle thickets" during his years of spiritual striving and had to overcome the fear that he felt while living in the forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199215_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199215-182">[166]</a></sup> The Nikaya-texts also narrate that the ascetic Gautama practised under two teachers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yogic" title="Yogic">yogic</a> meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya197195_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya197195-183">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaumakis20088_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaumakis20088-184">[168]</a></sup> According to the <i>Ariyapariyesanā-sutta</i> (MN 26) and its Chinese parallel at MĀ 204, after having mastered the teaching of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alara_Kalama" title="Alara Kalama">Ārāḍa Kālāma</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_language" title="Pali language">Pali</a>: <span lang="pi">Alara Kalama</span>), who taught a meditation attainment called "the sphere of nothingness", he was asked by Ārāḍa to become an equal leader of their spiritual community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200347_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200347-185">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011175_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011175-186">[170]</a></sup> However, Gautama felt unsatisfied by the practice because it "does not lead to revulsion, to dispassion, to cessation, to calm, to knowledge, to awakening, to Nibbana", and moved on to become a student of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Udaka_Ramaputta" title="Udaka Ramaputta">Udraka Rāmaputra</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_language" title="Pali language">Pali</a>: <span lang="pi">Udaka Ramaputta</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann200348_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann200348-187">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong200077_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong200077-188">[172]</a></sup> With him, he achieved high levels of meditative consciousness (called "The Sphere of Neither Perception nor Non-Perception") and was again asked to join his teacher. But, once more, he was not satisfied for the same reasons as before, and moved on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENarada199219–20_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENarada199219–20-189">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to some sutras, after leaving his meditation teachers, Gotama then practiced ascetic techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199026_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa199026-190">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[note 17]</a></sup> The ascetic techniques described in the early texts include very minimal food intake, different forms of <a href="/wiki/Pranayama" title="Pranayama">breath control</a>, and forceful mind control. The texts report that he became so emaciated that his bones became visible through his skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011236_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011236-193">[176]</a></sup> The <i>Mahāsaccaka-sutta</i> and most of its parallels agree that after taking asceticism to its extremes, Gautama realized that this had not helped him attain nirvana, and that he needed to regain strength to pursue his goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011240_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011240-194">[177]</a></sup> One popular story tells of how he accepted milk and <a href="/wiki/Rice_pudding" title="Rice pudding">rice pudding</a> from a village girl named <a href="/wiki/Sujata_(milkmaid)" title="Sujata (milkmaid)">Sujata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[178]</a></sup> His break with asceticism is said to have led his five companions to abandon him, since they believed that he had abandoned his search and become undisciplined. At this point, Gautama remembered a previous experience of <i>dhyana</i> he had as a child sitting under a tree while his father worked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011240_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011240-194">[177]</a></sup> This memory leads him to understand that <i>dhyana</i> ("meditation") is the path to liberation, and the texts then depict the Buddha achieving all four dhyanas, followed by the "three higher knowledges" (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tevijja" title="Tevijja">tevijja</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[note 18]</a></sup> culminating in complete insight into the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a>, thereby attaining <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">liberation</a> from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samsara" title="Samsara">samsara</a></i>, the endless cycle of rebirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011243_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011243-198">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson1999_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson1999-199">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams200274–75_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams200274–75-200">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-DL_Four_Truths_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-DL_Four_Truths-201">[183]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[note 19]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>Dhammacakkappavattana Sutta</i> (SN 56),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn56_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn56-203">[184]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tathagata" title="Tathagata">Tathagata</a>, the term Gautama uses most often to refer to himself, realized "the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Way" title="Middle Way">Middle Way</a>"—a path of moderation away from the extremes of <a href="/wiki/Hedonism" title="Hedonism">self-indulgence</a> and self-mortification, or the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn56_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-sn56-203">[184]</a></sup> In later centuries, Gautama became known as the <i>Buddha</i> or "Awakened One". The title indicates that unlike most people who are "asleep", a Buddha is understood as having "woken up" to the true nature of reality and sees the world 'as it is' (<i>yatha-bhutam</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin19988_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin19988-16">[8]</a></sup> A Buddha has achieved liberation (<i>vimutti</i>), also called Nirvana, which is seen as the extinguishing of the "fires" of desire, hatred, and ignorance, that keep the cycle of suffering and rebirth going.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following his decision to leave his meditation teachers, MĀ 204 and other parallel early texts report that Gautama sat down with the determination not to get up until full awakening (<i>sammā-sambodhi</i>) had been reached; the <i>Ariyapariyesanā-sutta</i> does not mention "full awakening", but only that he attained nirvana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011178_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011178-205">[186]</a></sup> This event was said to have occurred under a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_fig" title="Sacred fig">pipal</a> tree—known as "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bodhi_tree" title="Bodhi tree">Bodhi tree</a>"—in <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGyatso20078–9_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGyatso20078–9-206">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>As reported by various texts from the Pali Canon, the Buddha sat for seven days under the bodhi tree "feeling the bliss of deliverance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199230_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199230-207">[188]</a></sup> The Pali texts also report that he continued to meditate and contemplated various aspects of the Dharma while living by the <a href="/wiki/Lilajan_River" title="Lilajan River">River Nairañjanā</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Prat%C4%ABtyasamutp%C4%81da" title="Pratītyasamutpāda">Dependent Origination</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indriya" title="Indriya">Five Spiritual Faculties</a> and Suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199230–35_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199230–35-208">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>The legendary biographies like the <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81vastu" title="Mahāvastu">Mahavastu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nidanakatha" title="Nidanakatha">Nidanakatha</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Lalitavistara_S%C5%ABtra" title="Lalitavistara Sūtra">Lalitavistara</a></i> depict an attempt by <a href="/wiki/Mara_(demon)" title="Mara (demon)">Mara</a>, the ruler of the desire realm, to prevent the Buddha's nirvana. He does so by sending his daughters to seduce the Buddha, by asserting his superiority and by assaulting him with armies of monsters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200193_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200193-209">[190]</a></sup> However the Buddha is unfazed and calls on the earth (or in some versions of the legend, the <a href="/wiki/Prithvi" title="Prithvi">earth goddess</a>) as witness to his superiority by <a href="/wiki/Mudra" title="Mudra">touching the ground</a> before entering meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong200194_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong200194-210">[191]</a></sup> Other miracles and magical events are also depicted.
</p>
<h3><span id="First_sermon_and_formation_of_the_sa.E1.B9.85gha"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_sermon_and_formation_of_the_saṅgha">First sermon and formation of the saṅgha</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sarnath1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sarnath1.jpg/220px-Sarnath1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sarnath1.jpg/330px-Sarnath1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sarnath1.jpg/440px-Sarnath1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sarnath1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dhamek_Stupa" title="Dhamek Stupa">Dhamek Stupa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Sarnath</a>, India, site of the first teaching of the Buddha in which he taught the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a> to his first five disciples</div></div></div>
<p>According to MN 26, immediately after his awakening, the Buddha hesitated on whether or not he should teach the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharma_(Buddhism)" title="Dharma (Buddhism)">Dharma</a></i> to others. He was concerned that humans were overpowered by ignorance, greed, and hatred that it would be difficult for them to recognise the path, which is "subtle, deep and hard to grasp". However, the god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_(Buddhism)#Brahmā_Sahampati" title="Brahma (Buddhism)">Brahmā Sahampati</a> convinced him, arguing that at least some "with little dust in their eyes" will understand it. The Buddha relented and agreed to teach. According to Anālayo, the Chinese parallel to MN 26, MĀ 204, does not contain this story, but this event does appear in other parallel texts, such as in an <i><a href="/wiki/Ekottara_Agama" title="Ekottara Agama">Ekottarika-āgama</a></i> discourse, in the <i>Catusparisat-sūtra</i>, and in the <i>Lalitavistara</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011178_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011178-205">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to MN 26 and MĀ 204, after deciding to teach, the Buddha initially intended to visit his former teachers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alara_Kalama" title="Alara Kalama">Alara Kalama</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Udaka_Ramaputta" title="Udaka Ramaputta">Udaka Ramaputta</a>, to teach them his insights, but they had already died, so he decided to visit his five former companions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011182_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011182-211">[192]</a></sup> MN 26 and MĀ 204 both report that on his way to <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Vārānasī</a> (Benares), he met another wanderer, called Ājīvika Upaka in MN 26. The Buddha proclaimed that he had achieved full awakening, but Upaka was not convinced and "took a different path".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011183_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011183-212">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>MN 26 and MĀ 204 continue with the Buddha reaching the <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Deer Park (Sarnath)</a> (<i>Mrigadāva</i>, also called <i>Rishipatana</i>, "site where the ashes of the ascetics fell")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-213">[194]</a></sup> near Vārānasī, where he met the group of five ascetics and was able to convince them that he had indeed reached full awakening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011185_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011185-214">[195]</a></sup> According to MĀ 204 (but not MN 26), as well as the Theravāda Vinaya, an <a href="/wiki/Ekottara_Agama" title="Ekottara Agama"><i>Ekottarika-āgama</i></a> text, the Dharmaguptaka Vinaya, the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%AB%C5%9B%C4%81saka" title="Mahīśāsaka">Mahīśāsaka</a> Vinaya, and the <i>Mahāvastu</i>, the Buddha then taught them the "first sermon", also known as the "Benares sermon",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-213">[194]</a></sup> i.e. the teaching of "the noble eightfold path as the middle path aloof from the two extremes of sensual indulgence and self-mortification."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011185_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011185-214">[195]</a></sup> The Pali text reports that after the first sermon, the ascetic <a href="/wiki/Kaundinya" title="Kaundinya">Koṇḍañña</a> (Kaundinya) became the first <a href="/wiki/Arhat" title="Arhat">arahant</a> (liberated being) and the first Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">bhikkhu</a> or monastic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199244–45_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199244–45-215">[196]</a></sup> The Buddha then continued to teach the other ascetics and they formed the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sangha_(Buddhism)" title="Sangha (Buddhism)"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">saṅgha</i></span></a>: the company of Buddhist monks.
</p><p>Various sources such as the <i>Mahāvastu,</i> the <i>Mahākhandhaka</i> of the Theravāda Vinaya and the <i>Catusparisat-sūtra</i> also mention that the Buddha taught them his second discourse, about the characteristic of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">"not-self"</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Anattalakkha%E1%B9%87a_Sutta" title="Anattalakkhaṇa Sutta">Anātmalakṣaṇa Sūtra</a></i>), at this time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001110_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001110-216">[197]</a></sup> or five days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_213-2"><a href="#cite_note-:8-213">[194]</a></sup> After hearing this second sermon the four remaining ascetics also reached the status of <i>arahant.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_213-3"><a href="#cite_note-:8-213">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Theravāda Vinaya and the <i>Catusparisat-sūtra</i> also speak of the conversion of <a href="/wiki/Yasa" title="Yasa">Yasa</a>, a local guild master, and his friends and family, who were some of the first laypersons to be converted and to enter the Buddhist community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001113_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001113-217">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_213-4"><a href="#cite_note-:8-213">[194]</a></sup> The conversion of three brothers named Kassapa followed, who brought with them five hundred converts who had previously been "matted hair ascetics", and whose spiritual practice was related to fire sacrifices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199248,_54–59_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199248,_54–59-218">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001116–117_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001116–117-219">[200]</a></sup> According to the Theravāda Vinaya, the Buddha then stopped at the Gayasisa hill near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaya,_India" title="Gaya, India">Gaya</a> and delivered his third discourse, the <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80dittapariy%C4%81ya_Sutta" title="Ādittapariyāya Sutta">Ādittapariyāya Sutta</a></i> (The Discourse on Fire),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199264_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199264-220">[201]</a></sup> in which he taught that everything in the world is inflamed by passions and only those who follow the Eightfold path can be liberated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_213-5"><a href="#cite_note-:8-213">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of the rainy season, when the Buddha's community had grown to around sixty awakened monks, he instructed them to wander on their own, teach and ordain people into the community, for the "welfare and benefit" of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001115_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001115-221">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_213-6"><a href="#cite_note-:8-213">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="The_growth_of_the_sa.E1.B9.85gha"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_growth_of_the_saṅgha">The growth of the saṅgha</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG/220px-Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG/330px-Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG/440px-Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_mit_Mogallana_und_Sariputta.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The chief disciples of the Buddha, Mogallana (chief in psychic power) and Sariputta (chief in wisdom).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jetavana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1875" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jetavana.jpg/220px-Jetavana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jetavana.jpg/330px-Jetavana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jetavana.jpg/440px-Jetavana.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jetavana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The remains of a section of <a href="/wiki/Jetavana" title="Jetavana">Jetavana Monastery</a>, just outside of ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sravasti" title="Sravasti">Savatthi</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For the remaining 40 or 45 years of his life, the Buddha is said to have traveled in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gangetic_Plain" title="Gangetic Plain">Gangetic Plain</a>, in what is now <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, and southern Nepal, teaching a diverse range of people: from nobles to servants, ascetics and householders, murderers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angulimala" title="Angulimala">Angulimala</a>, and cannibals such as Alavaka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalalasekera1960291–292_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalalasekera1960291–292-222">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199815_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199815-162">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001131_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001131-223">[204]</a></sup> According to Schumann, the Buddha's wanderings ranged from "<a href="/wiki/Kosambi" title="Kosambi">Kosambi</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Yamuna" title="Yamuna">Yamuna</a> (25 km south-west of <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a> )", to Campa (40 km east of <a href="/wiki/Bhagalpur" title="Bhagalpur">Bhagalpur</a>)" and from "Kapilavatthu (95 km north-west of <a href="/wiki/Gorakhpur" title="Gorakhpur">Gorakhpur</a>) to Uruvela (south of Gaya)." This covers an area of 600 by 300 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003231_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003231-224">[205]</a></sup> His sangha enjoyed the patronage of the kings of <a href="/wiki/Kosala" title="Kosala">Kosala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> and he thus spent a lot of time in their respective capitals, <a href="/wiki/Shravasti" title="Shravasti">Savatthi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rajgir" title="Rajgir">Rajagaha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003231_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003231-224">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Buddha's language remains unknown, it is likely that he taught in one or more of a variety of closely related <a href="/wiki/Middle_Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Middle Indo-Aryan languages">Middle Indo-Aryan</a> dialects, of which <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a> may be a standardisation.
</p><p>The sangha traveled through the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">subcontinent</a>, expounding the Dharma. This continued throughout the year, except during the four months of the <a href="/wiki/Vassa" title="Vassa">Vassa</a> rainy season when ascetics of all religions rarely traveled. One reason was that it was more difficult to do so without causing harm to flora and animal life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001132_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001132-225">[206]</a></sup> The health of the ascetics might have been a concern as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[207]</a></sup> At this time of year, the sangha would retreat to monasteries, public parks or forests, where people would come to them.
</p><p>The first vassana was spent at <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Varanasi</a> when the sangha was formed. According to the Pali texts, shortly after the formation of the sangha, the Buddha traveled to <a href="/wiki/Rajgir" title="Rajgir">Rajagaha</a>, capital of <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a>, and met with King <a href="/wiki/Bimbisara" title="Bimbisara">Bimbisara</a>, who gifted a bamboo grove park to the sangha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199268_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199268-227">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha's sangha continued to grow during his initial travels in north India. The early texts tell the story of how the Buddha's <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Ar%C4%81vaka#Foremost_disciples" title="Śrāvaka">chief disciples</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C4%81riputra" title="Śāriputra">Sāriputta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maudgalyayana" title="Maudgalyayana">Mahāmoggallāna</a>, who were both students of the skeptic sramana <a href="/wiki/Sanjaya_Belatthiputta" title="Sanjaya Belatthiputta">Sañjaya Belaṭṭhiputta</a>, were converted by <a href="/wiki/Assaji" title="Assaji">Assaji</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199270_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199270-228">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001119_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001119-229">[210]</a></sup> They also tell of how the Buddha's son, <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81hula" title="Rāhula">Rahula</a>, joined his father as a bhikkhu when the Buddha visited his old home, Kapilavastu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199278_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199278-230">[211]</a></sup> Over time, other Shakyans joined the order as bhikkhus, such as Buddha's cousin <a href="/wiki/%C4%80nanda" title="Ānanda">Ananda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anuruddha" title="Anuruddha">Anuruddha</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upali" title="Upali">Upali</a> the barber, the Buddha's half-brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanda_(Buddhism)" title="Nanda (Buddhism)">Nanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devadatta" title="Devadatta">Devadatta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199279–83_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199279–83-231">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001122_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001122-232">[213]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the Buddha's father Suddhodana heard his son's teaching, converted to Buddhism and became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sotapanna" title="Sotapanna">stream-enterer</a>.
</p><p>The early texts also mention an important lay disciple, the merchant <a href="/wiki/Anathapindika" title="Anathapindika">Anāthapiṇḍika</a>, who became a strong lay supporter of the Buddha early on. He is said to have gifted <a href="/wiki/Jetavana" title="Jetavana">Jeta's grove</a> (<i>Jetavana</i>) to the sangha at great expense (the Theravada Vinaya speaks of thousands of gold coins).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199291_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu199291-233">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001136_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001136-234">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Formation_of_the_bhikkhun.C4.AB_order"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_of_the_bhikkhunī_order">Formation of the bhikkhunī order</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mahapajapati.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Mahapajapati.jpg/220px-Mahapajapati.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Mahapajapati.jpg/330px-Mahapajapati.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Mahapajapati.jpg/440px-Mahapajapati.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mahapajapati.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mahāprajāpatī, the first bhikkuni and Buddha's stepmother, ordains</div></div></div>
<p>The formation of a parallel order of female monastics (<a href="/wiki/Bhikkhun%C4%AB" title="Bhikkhunī">bhikkhunī</a>) was another important part of the growth of the Buddha's community. As noted by Anālayo's comparative study of this topic, there are various versions of this event depicted in the different early Buddhist texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[note 20]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to all the major versions surveyed by Anālayo, <a href="/wiki/Mahapajapati_Gotami" title="Mahapajapati Gotami">Mahāprajāpatī Gautamī</a>, Buddha's step-mother, is initially turned down by the Buddha after requesting ordination for her and some other women. Mahāprajāpatī and her followers then shave their hair, don robes and begin following the Buddha on his travels. The Buddha is eventually convinced by Ānanda to grant ordination to Mahāprajāpatī on her acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Eight_Garudhammas" title="Eight Garudhammas">eight conditions called gurudharmas</a> which focus on the relationship between the new order of nuns and the monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo201643_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo201643-237">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Anālayo, the only argument common to all the versions that Ananda uses to convince the Buddha is that women have the same ability to reach all stages of awakening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo201679_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo201679-238">[218]</a></sup> Anālayo also notes that some modern scholars have questioned the authenticity of the eight gurudharmas in their present form due to various inconsistencies. He holds that the historicity of the current lists of eight is doubtful, but that they may have been based on earlier injunctions by the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013b_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013b-239">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2016111–112_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2016111–112-240">[220]</a></sup> Anālayo also notes that various passages indicate that the reason for the Buddha's hesitation to ordain women was the danger that the life of a wandering sramana posed for women that were not under the protection of their male family members (such as dangers of sexual assault and abduction). Due to this, the gurudharma injunctions may have been a way to place "the newly founded order of nuns in a relationship to its male counterparts that resembles as much as possible the protection a laywoman could expect from her male relatives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2016127_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2016127-241">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_(9217704485).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_%289217704485%29.jpg/220px-Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_%289217704485%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_%289217704485%29.jpg/330px-Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_%289217704485%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_%289217704485%29.jpg/440px-Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_%289217704485%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Ajatasattu_worships_the_Buddha_(9217704485).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ajatashatru worships the Buddha, relief from the <a href="/wiki/Bharhut" title="Bharhut">Bharhut</a> Stupa at the Indian Museum, <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to J.S. Strong, after the first 20 years of his teaching career, the Buddha seems to have slowly settled in Sravasti, the capital of the Kingdom of Kosala, spending most of his later years in this city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001136_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001136-234">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the sangha grew in size, the need for a standardized set of monastic rules arose and the Buddha seems to have developed a set of regulations for the sangha. These are preserved in various texts called "<a href="/wiki/Pratimok%E1%B9%A3a" title="Pratimokṣa">Pratimoksa</a>" which were recited by the community every fortnight. The Pratimoksa includes general ethical precepts, as well as rules regarding the essentials of monastic life, such as bowls and robes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001134_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001134-242">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his later years, the Buddha's fame grew and he was invited to important royal events, such as the inauguration of the new council hall of the Shakyans (as seen in MN 53) and the inauguration of a new palace by Prince Bodhi (as depicted in MN 85).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003232–233_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003232–233-243">[223]</a></sup> The early texts also speak of how during the Buddha's old age, the kingdom of Magadha was usurped by a new king, <a href="/wiki/Ajatashatru" title="Ajatashatru">Ajatashatru</a>, who overthrew his father <a href="/wiki/Bimbisara" title="Bimbisara">Bimbisara</a>. According to the <i>Samaññaphala Sutta,</i> the new king spoke with different ascetic teachers and eventually took refuge in the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJain199179_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJain199179-244">[224]</a></sup> However, Jain sources also claim his allegiance, and it is likely he supported various religious groups, not just the Buddha's sangha exclusively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the Buddha continued to travel and teach, he also came into contact with <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a#Pre-Buddhist_śrāmana_schools_in_Buddhist_texts" title="Śramaṇa">members of other śrāmana sects.</a> There is evidence from the early texts that the Buddha encountered some of these figures and critiqued their doctrines. The <i>Samaññaphala Sutta</i> identifies six such sects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003215_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003215-246">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early texts also depict the elderly Buddha as suffering from back pain. Several texts depict him delegating teachings to his chief disciples since his body now needed more rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003232_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003232-247">[227]</a></sup> However, the Buddha continued teaching well into his old age.
</p><p>One of the most troubling events during the Buddha's old age was <a href="/wiki/Devadatta" title="Devadatta">Devadatta</a>'s schism. Early sources speak of how the Buddha's cousin, Devadatta, attempted to take over leadership of the order and then left the sangha with several Buddhist monks and formed a rival sect. This sect is said to have also been supported by King Ajatashatru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011198_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011198-248">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992257_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992257-249">[229]</a></sup> The Pali texts also depict Devadatta as plotting to kill the Buddha, but these plans all fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003236_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003236-250">[230]</a></sup> They also depict the Buddha as sending his two chief disciples (Sariputta and Moggallana) to this schismatic community in order to convince the monks who left with Devadatta to return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003237_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003237-251">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>All the major early Buddhist Vinaya texts depict Devadatta as a divisive figure who attempted to split the Buddhist community, but they disagree on what issues he disagreed with the Buddha on. The <a href="/wiki/Sthavira_nik%C4%81ya" title="Sthavira nikāya">Sthavira</a> texts generally focus on "five points" which are seen as excessive ascetic practices, while the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81s%C4%81%E1%B9%83ghika" title="Mahāsāṃghika">Mahāsaṅghika</a> Vinaya speaks of a more comprehensive disagreement, which has Devadatta alter the discourses as well as monastic discipline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>At around the same time of Devadatta's schism, there was also war between Ajatashatru's Kingdom of Magadha, and Kosala, led by an elderly king Pasenadi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992280_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992280-253">[233]</a></sup> Ajatashatru seems to have been victorious, a turn of events the Buddha is reported to have regretted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003239_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003239-254">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_days_and_parinirvana">Last days and <i>parinirvana</i></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_(25595318747).jpg"><img alt="Metal relief" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2897" data-file-width="5496" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_%2825595318747%29.jpg/300px-040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_%2825595318747%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_%2825595318747%29.jpg/450px-040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_%2825595318747%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_%2825595318747%29.jpg/600px-040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_%2825595318747%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:040_Ananda_worships_Buddha_(25595318747).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This East Javanese relief depicts the Buddha in his final days, and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80nanda" title="Ānanda">Ānanda</a>, his chief attendant.</div></div></div>
<p>The main narrative of the Buddha's last days, death and the events following his death is contained in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahaparinibbana_Sutta" title="Mahaparinibbana Sutta">Mahaparinibbana Sutta</a></i> (DN 16) and its various parallels in Sanskrit, Chinese, and Tibetan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001165_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001165-255">[235]</a></sup> According to Anālayo, these include the Chinese Dirgha Agama 2, "Sanskrit fragments of the <i>Mahaparinirvanasutra"</i>, and "three discourses preserved as individual translations in Chinese".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2014_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2014-256">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Mahaparinibbana sutta</i> depicts the Buddha's last year as a time of war. It begins with Ajatashatru's decision to make war on the <a href="/wiki/Vajjika_League" title="Vajjika League">Vajjika League</a>, leading him to send a minister to ask the Buddha for advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992286–288_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992286–288-257">[237]</a></sup> The Buddha responds by saying that the Vajjikas can be expected to prosper as long as they do seven things, and he then applies these seven principles to the Buddhist Sangha, showing that he is concerned about its future welfare. The Buddha says that the Sangha will prosper as long as they "hold regular and frequent assemblies, meet in harmony, do not change the rules of training, honor their superiors who were ordained before them, do not fall prey to worldly desires, remain devoted to forest hermitages, and preserve their personal mindfulness." He then gives further lists of important virtues to be upheld by the Sangha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001165–166_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001165–166-258">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early texts also depict how the Buddha's two chief disciples, Sariputta and Moggallana, died just before the Buddha's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003244_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003244-259">[239]</a></sup> The <i>Mahaparinibbana</i> depicts the Buddha as experiencing illness during the last months of his life but initially recovering. It also depicts him as stating that he cannot promote anyone to be his successor. When Ānanda requested this, the <i>Mahaparinibbana</i> records his response as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003246_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003246-260">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Ananda, why does the Order of monks expect this of me? I have taught the Dhamma, making no distinction of "inner" and " outer": the Tathagata has no "teacher's fist" (in which certain truths are held back). If there is anyone who thinks: "I shall take charge of the Order", or "the Order is under my leadership", such a person would have to make arrangements about the Order. The Tathagata does not think in such terms. Why should the Tathagata make arrangements for the Order? I am now old, worn out … I have reached the term of life, I am turning eighty years of age. Just as an old cart is made to go by being held together with straps, so the Tathagata's body is kept going by being bandaged up … Therefore, Ananda, you should live as islands unto yourselves, being your own refuge, seeking no other refuge; with the Dhamma as an island, with the Dhamma as your refuge, seeking no other refuge… Those monks who in my time or afterwards live thus, seeking an island and a refuge in themselves and in the Dhamma and nowhere else, these zealous ones are truly my monks and will overcome the darkness (of rebirth).</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dying_Buddha_(Mahaparinirvana),_Gandhara,_3rd_or_4th_century_AD,_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota,_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Dying_Buddha_%28Mahaparinirvana%29%2C_Gandhara%2C_3rd_or_4th_century_AD%2C_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota%2C_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg/220px-Dying_Buddha_%28Mahaparinirvana%29%2C_Gandhara%2C_3rd_or_4th_century_AD%2C_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota%2C_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Dying_Buddha_%28Mahaparinirvana%29%2C_Gandhara%2C_3rd_or_4th_century_AD%2C_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota%2C_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg/330px-Dying_Buddha_%28Mahaparinirvana%29%2C_Gandhara%2C_3rd_or_4th_century_AD%2C_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota%2C_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Dying_Buddha_%28Mahaparinirvana%29%2C_Gandhara%2C_3rd_or_4th_century_AD%2C_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota%2C_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg/440px-Dying_Buddha_%28Mahaparinirvana%29%2C_Gandhara%2C_3rd_or_4th_century_AD%2C_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota%2C_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dying_Buddha_(Mahaparinirvana),_Gandhara,_3rd_or_4th_century_AD,_gray_schist_-_John_and_Mable_Ringling_Museum_of_Art_-_Sarasota,_FL_-_DSC00665.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Parinirvana" title="Parinirvana">Mahaparinirvana</a>, Gandhara, 3rd or 4th century CE, gray schist</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage,_Cave_26_Ajanta,_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2839" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage%2C_Cave_26_Ajanta%2C_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg/220px-19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage%2C_Cave_26_Ajanta%2C_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage%2C_Cave_26_Ajanta%2C_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg/330px-19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage%2C_Cave_26_Ajanta%2C_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage%2C_Cave_26_Ajanta%2C_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg/440px-19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage%2C_Cave_26_Ajanta%2C_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:19th_century_sketch_and_21st_century_photo_collage,_Cave_26_Ajanta,_Buddha_Parinirvana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mahaparinibbana scene, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajanta_caves" title="Ajanta caves">Ajanta caves</a></div></div></div>
<p>After traveling and teaching some more, the Buddha ate his last meal, which he had received as an offering from a blacksmith named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cunda_(Buddhism)" title="Cunda (Buddhism)">Cunda</a>. Falling violently ill, Buddha instructed his attendant Ānanda to convince Cunda that the meal eaten at his place had nothing to do with his death and that his meal would be a source of the greatest merit as it provided the last meal for a Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[241]</a></sup> Bhikkhu and <a href="/wiki/Oskar_von_Hin%C3%BCber" title="Oskar von Hinüber">von Hinüber</a> argue that the Buddha died of <a href="/wiki/Superior_mesenteric_artery_syndrome" title="Superior mesenteric artery syndrome">mesenteric</a> <a href="/wiki/Infarction" title="Infarction">infarction</a>, a symptom of old age, rather than food poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhikkhuvon_Hinüber2000_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhikkhuvon_Hinüber2000-262">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>The precise contents of the Buddha's final meal are not clear, due to variant scriptural traditions and ambiguity over the translation of certain significant terms. The <a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada">Theravada</a> tradition generally believes that the Buddha was offered some kind of pork, while the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> tradition believes that the Buddha consumed some sort of truffle or other mushroom. These may reflect the different traditional views on <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_vegetarianism" title="Buddhist vegetarianism">Buddhist vegetarianism</a> and the precepts for monks and nuns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaley1932343–354_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaley1932343–354-264">[244]</a></sup> Modern scholars also disagree on this topic, arguing both for pig's flesh or some kind of plant or mushroom that pigs like to eat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wayley1932_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wayley1932-265">[note 21]</a></sup> Whatever the case, none of the sources which mention the last meal attribute the Buddha's sickness to the meal itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001176_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001176-266">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>As per the <i>Mahaparinibbana sutta,</i> after the meal with Cunda, the Buddha and his companions continued traveling until he was too weak to continue and had to stop at <a href="/wiki/Kushinagar" title="Kushinagar">Kushinagar</a>, where Ānanda had a resting place prepared in a grove of Sala trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003249_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003249-267">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001178_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001178-268">[247]</a></sup> After announcing to the sangha at large that he would soon be passing away to final Nirvana, the Buddha ordained one last novice into the order personally, his name was Subhadda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003249_267-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003249-267">[246]</a></sup> He then repeated his final instructions to the sangha, which was that the Dhamma and Vinaya was to be their teacher after his death. Then he asked if anyone had any doubts about the teaching, but nobody did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumann2003250_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumann2003250-269">[248]</a></sup> The Buddha's final words are reported to have been: "All <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%85kh%C4%81ra" title="Saṅkhāra"><i>saṅkhāras</i></a> decay. Strive for the goal with diligence (<i><a href="/wiki/Appam%C4%81da" title="Appamāda">appamāda</a></i>)" (Pali: 'vayadhammā saṅkhārā appamādena sampādethā').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne2007112_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne2007112-270">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001183_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001183-271">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>He then entered his final meditation and died, reaching what is known as <i>parinirvana</i> (final nirvana, the end of rebirth and suffering achieved after the death of the body). The <i>Mahaparinibbana</i> reports that in his final meditation he entered the four dhyanas consecutively, then the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyāna in Buddhism">immaterial attainments</a> and finally the meditative dwelling known as <i>nirodha-samāpatti,</i> before returning to the fourth dhyana right at the moment of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992324_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992324-272">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2001178_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2001178-268">[247]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa,_Kushinagar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa%2C_Kushinagar.jpg/220px-Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa%2C_Kushinagar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa%2C_Kushinagar.jpg/330px-Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa%2C_Kushinagar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa%2C_Kushinagar.jpg/440px-Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa%2C_Kushinagar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha%27s_cremation_stupa,_Kushinagar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buddha's cremation stupa, <a href="/wiki/Kushinagar" title="Kushinagar">Kushinagar</a> (Kushinara).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1454" data-file-width="958" decoding="async" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg/220px-Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg/330px-Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg/440px-Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piprawa_vase_with_relics_of_the_Buddha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Piprahwa" title="Piprahwa">Piprahwa</a> vase with relics of the Buddha. The inscription reads: <i>...salilanidhane Budhasa Bhagavate...</i> (<a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brahmi script</a>: ...𑀲𑀮𑀺𑀮𑀦𑀺𑀥𑀸𑀦𑁂 𑀩𑀼𑀥𑀲 𑀪𑀕𑀯𑀢𑁂...) "Relics of the Buddha Lord".</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_events">Posthumous events</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aar%C4%ABra" title="Śarīra">Śarīra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Relics_associated_with_Buddha" title="Relics associated with Buddha">Relics associated with Buddha</a></div>
<p>According to the <i>Mahaparinibbana sutta,</i> the Mallians of Kushinagar spent the days following the Buddha's death honoring his body with flowers, music and scents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992327_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992327-273">[252]</a></sup> The sangha waited until the eminent elder <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81k%C4%81%C5%9Byapa" title="Mahākāśyapa">Mahākassapa</a> arrived to pay his respects before cremating the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992330_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992330-274">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha's body was then cremated and the remains, including his bones, were kept as <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> and they were distributed among various north Indian kingdoms like Magadha, Shakya and <a href="/wiki/Koliya" title="Koliya">Koliya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992331_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÑāṇamoli_Bhikkhu1992331-275">[254]</a></sup> These relics were placed in monuments or mounds called <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupas</a>, a common funerary practice at the time. Centuries later they would be exhumed and enshrined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Ashoka" title="King Ashoka">Ashoka</a> into many new stupas around the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Mauryan realm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2007136–137_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2007136–137-277">[256]</a></sup> Many supernatural legends surround the history of alleged relics as they accompanied the spread of Buddhism and gave legitimacy to rulers.
</p><p>According to various Buddhist sources, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Buddhist_Council" title="First Buddhist Council">First Buddhist Council</a> was held shortly after the Buddha's death to collect, recite and memorize the teachings. Mahākassapa was chosen by the sangha to be the chairman of the council. However, the historicity of the traditional accounts of the first council is disputed by modern scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings">Teachings</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy#The_Buddha_and_early_Buddhism" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosophy § The Buddha and early Buddhism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tracing_the_oldest_teachings">Tracing the oldest teachings</span></h3>
<p>One method to obtain information on the oldest core of Buddhism is to compare the oldest versions of the <a href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a> and other texts, such as the surviving portions of <a href="/wiki/Sarvastivada" title="Sarvastivada">Sarvastivada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mulasarvastivada" title="Mulasarvastivada">Mulasarvastivada</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahisasaka" title="Mahisasaka">Mahisasaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix-279">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder2000[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_280-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder2000[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-280'>[259]</a></sup> and the Chinese <a href="/wiki/%C4%80gama_(Buddhism)" title="Āgama (Buddhism)">Agamas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[261]</a></sup> The reliability of these sources, and the possibility of drawing out a core of oldest teachings, is a matter of dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-283">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988-284">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1990_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1990-285">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997-286">[265]</a></sup> According to Tilmann Vetter, inconsistencies remain, and other methods must be applied to resolve those inconsistencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix-279">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[note 22]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Lambert_Schmithausen" title="Lambert Schmithausen">Lambert Schmithausen</a>, there are three positions held by modern scholars of Buddhism:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993vii_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993vii-290">[268]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>"Stress on the fundamental homogeneity and substantial authenticity of at least a considerable part of the Nikayic materials."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[note 23]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Scepticism with regard to the possibility of retrieving the doctrine of earliest Buddhism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[note 24]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Cautious optimism in this respect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[note 25]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Regarding their attribution to the historical Buddha Gautama "Sakyamuni", scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gombrich" title="Richard Gombrich">Richard Gombrich</a>, Akira Hirakawa, Alexander Wynne and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.K._Warder" title="A.K. Warder">A.K. Warder</a> hold that these Early Buddhist Texts contain material that could possibly be traced to this figure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997_286-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997-286">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder2004[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_298-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder2004[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(October_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-298'>[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-164">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W,_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_(11249495494).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1545" data-file-width="3769" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W%2C_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_%2811249495494%29.jpg/220px-Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W%2C_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_%2811249495494%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W%2C_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_%2811249495494%29.jpg/330px-Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W%2C_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_%2811249495494%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W%2C_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_%2811249495494%29.jpg/440px-Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W%2C_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_%2811249495494%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_-_Lalitavistara_-_071_W,_The_Bodhisattva_meets_with_Arada_Kalama_(11249495494).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Bodhisattva meets with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alara_Kalama" title="Alara Kalama">Alara Kalama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">Borobudur</a> relief.</div></div></div>
<p>According to scholars of <a href="/wiki/Indology" title="Indology">Indology</a> such as Richard Gombrich, the Buddha's teachings on <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">Karma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rebirth_(Buddhism)" title="Rebirth (Buddhism)">Rebirth</a> are a development of pre-Buddhist themes that can be found in <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahminism" title="Brahminism">Brahmanical</a> sources, like the <i><a href="/wiki/Brihadaranyaka_Upanishad" title="Brihadaranyaka Upanishad">Brihadaranyaka Upanishad</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich20099,_36_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich20099,_36-299">[274]</a></sup> Likewise, <i><a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra" title="Saṃsāra">samsara</a></i>, the idea that we are trapped in cycles of rebirth and that we should seek liberation from them through non-harming (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahi%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Ahiṃsā">ahimsa</a></i>) and spiritual practices, pre-dates the Buddha and was likely taught in early Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200948_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200948-300">[275]</a></sup>
</p><p>In various texts, the Buddha is depicted as having studied under two named teachers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alara_Kalama" title="Alara Kalama">Āḷāra Kālāma</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uddaka_Ramaputta" title="Uddaka Ramaputta">Uddaka Rāmaputta</a>. According to Alexander Wynne, these were yogis who taught doctrines and practices similar to those in the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne200423,_37_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne200423,_37-301">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha's tribe of origin, the Shakyas, also seem to have had non-Vedic religious practices which influenced Buddhism, such as the veneration of trees and sacred groves, and the worship of tree spirits (yakkhas) and serpent beings (nagas). They also seem to have built burial mounds called stupas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levman2013_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levman2013-77">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tree veneration remains important in Buddhism today, particularly in the practice of venerating Bodhi trees. Likewise, yakkas and nagas have remained important figures in Buddhist religious practices and mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levman2013_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levman2013-77">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Early Buddhist Texts, the Buddha also references Brahmanical devices. For example, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samyutta_Nikaya" title="Samyutta Nikaya">Samyutta Nikaya</a> 111, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majjhima_Nikaya" title="Majjhima Nikaya">Majjhima Nikaya</a> 92 and Vinaya i 246 of the <a href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a>, the Buddha praises the <a href="/wiki/Agnihotra" title="Agnihotra">Agnihotra</a> as the foremost sacrifice and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gayatri_mantra" title="Gayatri mantra">Gayatri mantra</a> as the foremost meter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[note 26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddhist teaching of the <i><a href="/wiki/Three_marks_of_existence" title="Three marks of existence">three marks of existence</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[note 27]</a></sup> may also reflect Upanishadic or other influences according to K.R. Norman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman199726_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman199726-305">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Johannes Bronkhorst, the "meditation without breath and reduced intake of food" which the Buddha practiced before his awakening are forms of asceticism which are similar to Jain practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst199310_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst199310-306">[279]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddhist practice called <a href="/wiki/Brahmavihara" title="Brahmavihara"><i>Brahma-vihara</i></a> may have also originated from a Brahmanic term;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman199728_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman199728-307">[280]</a></sup> but its usage may have been common in the sramana traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_283-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-283">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings_preserved_in_the_Early_Buddhist_Texts">Teachings preserved in the Early Buddhist Texts</span></h3>
