<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_(1st_C),_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_%281st_C%29%2C_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg/220px-Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_%281st_C%29%2C_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_%281st_C%29%2C_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg/330px-Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_%281st_C%29%2C_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_%281st_C%29%2C_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg/440px-Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_%281st_C%29%2C_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fragmentary_Buddhist_text_-_Gandhara_birchbark_scrolls_(1st_C),_part_31_-_BL_Or._14915.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gandharan_Buddhism" title="Gandharan Buddhism">Gandharan Buddhist</a> birchbark scroll fragments</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_Texts" title="Early Buddhist Texts">Early Buddhist Texts</a></div><p>The Early Buddhist Texts present many teachings and practices which may have been taught by the historical Buddha. These include basic doctrines such as <a href="/wiki/Prat%C4%ABtyasamutp%C4%81da" title="Pratītyasamutpāda">Dependent Origination</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Way" title="Middle Way">Middle Way</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Skandha" title="Skandha">Five Aggregates</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Three_poisons" title="Three poisons">Three unwholesome roots</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Eightfold Path</a>. According to N. Ross Reat, all of these doctrines are shared by the Theravada Pali texts and the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81s%C4%81%E1%B9%83ghika" title="Mahāsāṃghika">Mahasamghika</a> school's <a href="/wiki/Salistamba_Sutra" title="Salistamba Sutra"><i>Śālistamba Sūtra</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[281]</a></sup>
</p><p>A recent study by <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Analayo" title="Bhikkhu Analayo">Bhikkhu Analayo</a> concludes that the Theravada <i><a href="/wiki/Majjhima_Nik%C4%81ya" title="Majjhima Nikāya">Majjhima Nikaya</a></i> and Sarvastivada <i><a href="/wiki/Madhyama_Agama" title="Madhyama Agama">Madhyama Agama</a></i> contain mostly the same major doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011891_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011891-309">[282]</a></sup> Likewise, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Salomon_(South_Asian_Studies)" title="Richard Salomon (South Asian Studies)">Richard Salomon</a> has written that the doctrines found in the <a href="/wiki/Gandh%C4%81ran_Buddhist_texts" title="Gandhāran Buddhist texts">Gandharan Manuscripts</a> are "consistent with non-Mahayana Buddhism, which survives today in the Theravada school of Sri Lanka and Southeast Asia, but which in ancient times was represented by eighteen separate schools."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[283]</a></sup>
</p><p>These basic teachings such as the Four Noble Truths tend to be widely accepted as basic doctrines in all major <a href="/wiki/Schools_of_Buddhism" title="Schools of Buddhism">schools of Buddhism</a>, as seen in ecumenical documents such as the 1967 statement <i><a href="/wiki/Basic_points_unifying_Therav%C4%81da_and_Mah%C4%81y%C4%81na" title="Basic points unifying Theravāda and Mahāyāna">Basic points unifying Theravāda and Mahāyāna</a>.</i>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Critique_of_Brahmanism">Critique of Brahmanism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_(9218121775).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4443" data-file-width="2377" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_%289218121775%29.jpg/170px-Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_%289218121775%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_%289218121775%29.jpg/255px-Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_%289218121775%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_%289218121775%29.jpg/340px-Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_%289218121775%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Museum_Sculpture_-_Buddha_meets_a_Brahmin_(9218121775).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buddha meets a Brahmin, at the Indian Museum, <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the early Buddhist texts, the Buddha critiques the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Brahmanical religion</a> and social system on certain key points.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmin</a> caste held that the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> were eternal revealed (<i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aruti" title="Śruti">sruti</a></i>) texts. The Buddha, on the other hand, did not accept that these texts had any divine authority or value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tola&amp;Dragonetti_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tola&amp;Dragonetti-311">[284]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha also did not see the Brahmanical rites and practices as useful for spiritual advancement. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Ud%C4%81na" title="Udāna">Udāna</a>, the Buddha points out that <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual bathing</a> does not lead to purity, only "truth and morality" lead to purity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[note 28]</a></sup> He especially critiqued <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">animal sacrifice</a> as taught in Vedas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tola&amp;Dragonetti_311-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tola&amp;Dragonetti-311">[284]</a></sup> The Buddha contrasted his teachings, which were taught openly to all people, with that of the Brahmins', who kept their <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a> secret.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[note 29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha also critiqued the Brahmins' claims of superior birth and the idea that different castes and bloodlines were inherently pure or impure, noble or ignoble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tola&amp;Dragonetti_311-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tola&amp;Dragonetti-311">[284]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>Vasettha sutta </i>the Buddha argues that the main difference among humans is not birth but their actions and occupations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmvedt200376_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmvedt200376-315">[286]</a></sup> According to the Buddha, one is a "Brahmin" (i.e. divine, like <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>) only to the extent that one has cultivated virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[note 30]</a></sup> Because of this the early texts report that he proclaimed: "Not by birth one is a Brahman, not by birth one is a non-Brahman; - by moral action one is a Brahman"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tola&amp;Dragonetti_311-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tola&amp;Dragonetti-311">[284]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Agga%C3%B1%C3%B1a_Sutta" title="Aggañña Sutta">Aggañña Sutta</a></i> explains all classes or <a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">varnas</a> can be good or bad and gives a sociological explanation for how they arose, against the Brahmanical idea that they are divinely ordained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOmvedt200372_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOmvedt200372-317">[287]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Kancha_Ilaiah" title="Kancha Ilaiah">Kancha Ilaiah</a>, the Buddha posed the first <a href="/wiki/Contract_theory" title="Contract theory">contract theory</a> of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[288]</a></sup> The Buddha's teaching then is a single universal moral law, one <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a> valid for everybody, which is opposed to the Brahmanic ethic founded on "one's own duty" (<i>svadharma</i>) which depends on caste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tola&amp;Dragonetti_311-6"><a href="#cite_note-Tola&amp;Dragonetti-311">[284]</a></sup> Because of this, all castes including untouchables were welcome in the Buddhist order and when someone joined, they renounced all caste affiliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[290]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_of_existence">Analysis of existence</span></h4>
<p>The early Buddhist texts present the Buddha's worldview as focused on understanding the nature of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">dukkha</a></i>, which is seen as the fundamental problem of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199859_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199859-321">[291]</a></sup> Dukkha refers to all kinds of suffering, unease, frustration, and dissatisfaction that sentient beings experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199861_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199861-323">[293]</a></sup> At the core of the Buddha's analysis of dukkha is the fact that everything we experience is impermanent, unstable and thus unreliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199862_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199862-324">[294]</a></sup>
</p><p>A common presentation of the core structure of Buddha's teaching found in the early texts is that of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199863–64_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199863–64-325">[295]</a></sup> This teaching is most famously presented in the <i><a href="/wiki/Dhammacakkappavattana_Sutta" title="Dhammacakkappavattana Sutta">Dhammacakkappavattana Sutta</a></i> ("The discourse on the turning of the Dharma wheel") and its many parallels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013a_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013a-326">[296]</a></sup> The basic outline of the four truths is as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199863–64_325-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199863–64-325">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>There is dukkha.</li>
<li>There are causes and conditions for the arising of dukkha. Various conditions are outlined in the early texts, such as <a href="/wiki/Ta%E1%B9%87h%C4%81" title="Taṇhā">craving (<i>taṇhā</i>)</a>, but the three most basic ones are <a href="/wiki/Three_poisons" title="Three poisons">greed, aversion and delusion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199872–73_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199872–73-327">[297]</a></sup></li>
<li>If the conditions for dukkha cease, dukkha also ceases. This is "<a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">Nirvana</a>" (literally 'blowing out' or 'extinguishing').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199875_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199875-328">[298]</a></sup></li>
<li>There is path to follow that leads to Nirvana.</li></ul>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Analayo" title="Bhikkhu Analayo">Bhikkhu Analayo</a>, the four truths schema appears to be based "on an analogy with <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Indian medical</a> diagnosis" (with the form: "disease, pathogen, health, cure") and this comparison is "explicitly made in several early Buddhist texts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013a_326-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013a-326">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>In another Pali sutta, the Buddha outlines how "eight worldly conditions", "keep the world turning around...Gain and loss, fame and disrepute, praise and blame, pleasure and pain." He then explains how the difference between a noble (<i>arya</i>) person and an uninstructed worldling is that a noble person reflects on and understands the impermanence of these conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi200532–33_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi200532–33-329">[299]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha's analysis of existence includes an understanding that <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Buddhism" title="Karma in Buddhism">karma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rebirth_(Buddhism)" title="Rebirth (Buddhism)">rebirth</a> are part of life. According to the Buddha, the constant cycle of dying and being reborn (i.e. saṃsāra) according to one's karma is just dukkha and the ultimate spiritual goal should be liberation from this cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200912_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200912-330">[300]</a></sup> According to the Pali suttas, the Buddha stated that "this saṃsāra is without discoverable beginning. A first point is not discerned of beings roaming and wandering on hindered by ignorance and fettered by craving."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi200539_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi200539-331">[301]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha's teaching of karma differed to that of the Jains and Brahmins, in that on his view, karma is primarily mental intention (as opposed to mainly physical action or ritual acts).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup> The Buddha is reported to have said "By karma I mean intention."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200949_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200949-332">[302]</a></sup> Richard Gombrich summarizes the Buddha's view of karma as follows: "all thoughts, words, and deeds derive their moral value, positive or negative, from the intention behind them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200913_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200913-333">[303]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the Buddha, our karmic acts also affected the rebirth process in a positive or negative way. This was seen as an impersonal natural law similar to how certain seeds produce certain plants and fruits (in fact, the result of a karmic act was called its "fruit" by the Buddha).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200919_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200919-334">[304]</a></sup> However, it is important to note that the Buddha did not hold that everything that happens is the result of karma alone. In fact when the Buddha was asked to state the causes of pain and pleasure he listed various physical and environmental causes alongside karma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200920_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200920-335">[305]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dependent_Origination">Dependent Origination</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_teaching_Dharma,_on_lion_throne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3898" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Buddha_teaching_Dharma%2C_on_lion_throne.jpg/170px-Buddha_teaching_Dharma%2C_on_lion_throne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Buddha_teaching_Dharma%2C_on_lion_throne.jpg/255px-Buddha_teaching_Dharma%2C_on_lion_throne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Buddha_teaching_Dharma%2C_on_lion_throne.jpg/340px-Buddha_teaching_Dharma%2C_on_lion_throne.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_teaching_Dharma,_on_lion_throne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Schist" title="Schist">Schist</a> Buddha statue with the famed <a href="/wiki/Ye_Dharma_Hetu" title="Ye Dharma Hetu">Ye Dharma Hetu</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dh%C4%81ra%E1%B9%87%C4%AB" title="Dhāraṇī">dhāraṇī</a> around the head, which was used as a common summary of Dependent Origination. It states: "Of those experiences that arise from a cause, The Tathāgata has said: 'this is their cause, And this is their cessation': This is what the Great Śramaṇa teaches."</div></div></div>
<p>In the early texts, the process of the arising of dukkha is most thoroughly explained by the Buddha through the teaching of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dependent_Origination" title="Dependent Origination">Dependent Origination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup> At its most basic level, Dependent Origination is an empirical teaching on the nature of phenomena which says that nothing is experienced independently of its conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton200022_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton200022-336">[306]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most basic formulation of Dependent Origination is given in the early texts as: 'It being thus, this comes about' (Pali: <i>evam sati idam hoti</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009131_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009131-337">[307]</a></sup> This can be taken to mean that certain phenomena only arise when there are other phenomena present (example: when there is craving, suffering arises), and so, one can say that their arising is "dependent" on other phenomena. In other words, nothing in experience exists without a cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009131_337-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009131-337">[307]</a></sup>
</p><p>In numerous early texts, this basic principle is expanded with a list of phenomena that are said to be conditionally dependent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998141–142_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998141–142-338">[308]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[note 31]</a></sup> These phenomena are supposed to provide an analysis of the cycle of dukkha as experienced by sentient beings. The philosopher Mark Siderits has outlined the basic idea of the Buddha's teaching of Dependent Origination of dukkha as follows:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>given the existence of a fully functioning assemblage of psycho-physical elements (the parts that make up a sentient being), ignorance concerning the three characteristics of sentient existence—suffering, impermanence and non-self—will lead, in the course of normal interactions with the environment, to appropriation (the identification of certain elements as 'I' and 'mine'). This leads in turn to the formation of attachments, in the form of desire and aversion, and the strengthening of ignorance concerning the true nature of sentient existence. These ensure future rebirth, and thus future instances of old age, disease and death, in a potentially unending cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The Buddha saw his analysis of Dependent Origination as a "Middle Way" between "eternalism" (<i>sassatavada</i>, the idea that some essence exists eternally) and "annihilationism" (<i>ucchedavada</i>, the idea that we go completely out of existence at death).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009131_337-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009131-337">[307]</a></sup> This middle way is basically the view that, conventionally speaking, persons are just a causal series of impermanent psycho-physical elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics_and_personal_identity">Metaphysics and personal identity</span></h4>
<p>Closely connected to the idea that experience is dependently originated is the Buddha's teaching that there is no independent or permanent self (Sanskrit: <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)"><i>atman</i></a>, Pali: <i>atta</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton200022_336-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton200022-336">[306]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to this view (termed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">anatta</a></i>), the Buddha's teaching was opposed to all soul theories of his time, including the Jain theory of a <i>"<a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">jiva</a>"</i> ("life monad") and the Brahmanical theories of atman and <a href="/wiki/Purusha" title="Purusha">purusha</a>. All of these theories held that there was an eternal unchanging <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> to a person which transmigrated from life to life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton200020_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton200020-340">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200962–64_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200962–64-341">[310]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Brahminical teachers affirmed atman theories in an attempt to answer the question of what really exists ultimately, the Buddha saw this question as not being useful, as illustrated in <a href="/wiki/Parable_of_the_Poisoned_Arrow" title="Parable of the Poisoned Arrow">the parable of the poisoned arrow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton200023–25_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton200023–25-342">[311]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the Buddha's contemporaries, the atman was also seen to be the unchanging constant which was separate from all changing experiences and the inner controller in a person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998134–135_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998134–135-343">[312]</a></sup> The Buddha instead held that all things in the world of our experience are transient and that there is no unchanging part to a person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich20099,_67_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich20099,_67-344">[313]</a></sup> According to Richard Gombrich, the Buddha's position is simply that "everything is process".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200910_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200910-345">[314]</a></sup> However, this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-essentialism" title="Anti-essentialism">anti-essentialist</a> view still includes an understanding of continuity through rebirth, it is just the rebirth of a process (karma), not an essence like the atman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich200973–74_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich200973–74-346">[315]</a></sup>
</p><p>Perhaps the most important way the Buddha analyzed individual experience in the early texts was by way of the <a href="/wiki/Skandha" title="Skandha">five 'aggregates' or 'groups' (<i>khandha</i>)</a> of physical and mental processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998135_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998135-347">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009114_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009114-348">[317]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy#Anatta" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddha's arguments against an unchanging self</a> rely on these five aggregate schema, as can be seen in the Pali <i><a href="/wiki/Anattalakkha%E1%B9%87a_Sutta" title="Anattalakkhaṇa Sutta">Anattalakkhaṇa Sutta</a></i> (and its parallels in Gandhari and Chinese)<i>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton200019–20_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton200019–20-349">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[319]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[320]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the early texts, the Buddha argued that because we have no ultimate control over any of the psycho-physical processes that make up a person, there cannot be an "inner controller" with command over them. Also, since they are all impermanent, one cannot regard any of the psycho-physical processes as an unchanging self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998136–137_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998136–137-352">[321]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup> Even mental processes such as <a href="/wiki/Vij%C3%B1%C4%81na" title="Vijñāna">consciousness</a> and will (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetana" title="Cetana">cetana</a></i>) are seen as being dependently originated and impermanent and thus do not qualify as a self (<i>atman</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup>
</p><p>As noted by Gombrich, in the early texts the Buddha teaches that all five aggregates, including consciousness (<i><a href="/wiki/Vij%C3%B1%C4%81na" title="Vijñāna">viññana</a></i>, which was held by Brahmins to be eternal), arise dependent on causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009119–120_353-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009119–120-353">[322]</a></sup> That is, existence is based on processes that are subject to dependent origination. He compared samsaric existence to a fire, which is dynamic and requires fuel (the <i>khandas</i>, literally: "heaps") in order to keep burning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009124–125_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2009124–125-354">[323]</a></sup>
</p><p>As <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Gethin" title="Rupert Gethin">Rupert Gethin</a> explains, for the Buddha:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I am a complex flow of physical and mental phenomena, but peel away these phenomena and look behind them and one just does not find a constant self that one can call one's own. My sense of self is both logically and emotionally just a label that I impose on these physical and mental phenomena in consequence of their connectedness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998139_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998139-355">[324]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The Buddha saw the belief in a self as arising from our grasping at and identifying with the various changing phenomena, as well as from ignorance about how things really are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998146–147_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998146–147-356">[325]</a></sup> Furthermore, the Buddha held that we experience suffering because we hold on to erroneous self views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998148_357-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998148-357">[326]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton200027_358-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton200027-358">[327]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldly_happiness">Worldly happiness</span></h4>
<p>As noted by <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Bodhi" title="Bhikkhu Bodhi">Bhikkhu Bodhi</a>, the Buddha as depicted in the Pali suttas does not exclusively teach a world transcending goal, but also teaches laypersons how to achieve worldly <a href="/wiki/Happiness" title="Happiness">happiness</a> (<i>sukha</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005107–109_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005107–109-359">[328]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Bodhi, the "most comprehensive" of the suttas that focus on how to live as a layperson is the <i><a href="/wiki/Sig%C4%81lov%C4%81da_Sutta" title="Sigālovāda Sutta">Sigālovāda Sutta</a></i> (DN 31). This sutta outlines how a layperson behaves towards six basic social relationships: "parents and children, teacher and pupils, husband and wife, friend and friend, employer and workers, lay follower and religious guides."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005109_360-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005109-360">[329]</a></sup> This Pali text also has parallels in Chinese and in Sanskrit fragments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[330]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[331]</a></sup>
</p><p>In another sutta (<i><a href="/wiki/D%C4%ABghaj%C4%81%E1%B9%87u_Sutta" title="Dīghajāṇu Sutta">Dīghajāṇu Sutta</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/A%E1%B9%85guttara_Nik%C4%81ya" title="Aṅguttara Nikāya">AN</a> 8.54) the Buddha teaches two types of happiness. First, there is the happiness visible in this very life. The Buddha states that four things lead to this happiness: "The accomplishment of persistent effort, the accomplishment of protection, good friendship, and balanced living."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005124_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005124-363">[332]</a></sup> Similarly, in several other suttas, the Buddha teaches on how to improve family relationships, particularly on the importance of filial love and gratitude as well as marital well-being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005110_364-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005110-364">[333]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding the happiness of the next life, the Buddha (in the <i><a href="/wiki/D%C4%ABghaj%C4%81%E1%B9%87u_Sutta" title="Dīghajāṇu Sutta">Dīghajāṇu Sutta</a></i>) states that the virtues which lead to a good rebirth are: <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Buddhism" title="Faith in Buddhism">faith</a> (in the Buddha and the teachings), moral discipline, especially keeping the <a href="/wiki/Five_precepts" title="Five precepts">five precepts</a>, generosity, and wisdom (knowledge of the arising and passing of things).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005111,_125_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005111,_125-365">[334]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Buddha of the suttas then, achieving a good rebirth is based on cultivating wholesome or skillful (<i>kusala</i>) karma, which leads to a good result, and avoiding unwholesome (<i>akusala</i>) karma. A common list of good karmas taught by the Buddha is the list of ten courses of action (<i><a href="/wiki/Kammapatha" title="Kammapatha">kammapatha</a></i>) as outlined in MN 41 <i>Saleyyaka Sutta</i> (and its Chinese parallel in SĀ 1042).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005146–148,_156_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005146–148,_156-366">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011263_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011263-367">[336]</a></sup>
</p><p>Good karma is also termed <a href="/wiki/Merit_(Buddhism)" title="Merit (Buddhism)">merit (<i>puñña</i>)</a>, and the Buddha outlines three bases of meritorious actions: giving, moral discipline and meditation (as seen in AN 8:36).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005151,_167_368-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005151,_167-368">[337]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Path_to_Liberation">The Path to Liberation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seated_Buddha,_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan,_Ghandhara_region,_2nd_-_3rd_century,_gray_schist,_HAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2820" data-file-width="2154" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Seated_Buddha%2C_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan%2C_Ghandhara_region%2C_2nd_-_3rd_century%2C_gray_schist%2C_HAA.jpg/170px-Seated_Buddha%2C_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan%2C_Ghandhara_region%2C_2nd_-_3rd_century%2C_gray_schist%2C_HAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Seated_Buddha%2C_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan%2C_Ghandhara_region%2C_2nd_-_3rd_century%2C_gray_schist%2C_HAA.jpg/255px-Seated_Buddha%2C_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan%2C_Ghandhara_region%2C_2nd_-_3rd_century%2C_gray_schist%2C_HAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Seated_Buddha%2C_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan%2C_Ghandhara_region%2C_2nd_-_3rd_century%2C_gray_schist%2C_HAA.jpg/340px-Seated_Buddha%2C_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan%2C_Ghandhara_region%2C_2nd_-_3rd_century%2C_gray_schist%2C_HAA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seated_Buddha,_Pakistan_or_Afghanistan,_Ghandhara_region,_2nd_-_3rd_century,_gray_schist,_HAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gandharan" title="Gandharan">Gandharan</a> sculpture depicting the Buddha in the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_position" title="Lotus position">full lotus</a> seated meditation posture, 2nd–3rd century CE</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_(25144212713).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_%2825144212713%29.jpg/220px-20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_%2825144212713%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_%2825144212713%29.jpg/330px-20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_%2825144212713%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_%2825144212713%29.jpg/440px-20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_%2825144212713%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20160124_Sri_Lanka_3769_Polonnaruwa_sRGB_(25144212713).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buddha Statues from <a href="/wiki/Gal_Vihara" title="Gal Vihara">Gal Vihara</a>. The Early Buddhist texts also mention meditation practice while standing and lying down.</div></div></div>
<p>Liberation (<i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">vimutti</a></i>) from the ignorance and grasping which create suffering is not easily achieved because all beings have deeply entrenched habits (termed <i><a href="/wiki/Asava" title="Asava">āsavas</a></i>, often translated as "influxes" or "defilements") that keep them trapped in samsara. Because of this, the Buddha taught a path (<i>marga</i>) of training to undo such habits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiderits2019_322-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiderits2019-322">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005229_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005229-369">[338]</a></sup> This path taught by the Buddha is depicted in the early texts (most famously in the Pali <i><a href="/wiki/Dhammacakkappavattana_Sutta" title="Dhammacakkappavattana Sutta">Dhammacakkappavattana Sutta</a></i> and its numerous parallel texts) as a "<a href="/wiki/Middle_Way" title="Middle Way">Middle Way</a>" between sensual indulgence on one hand and mortification of the body on the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013a_326-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2013a-326">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most common formulations of the path to liberation in the earliest Buddhist texts is the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199881_370-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199881-370">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[note 32]</a></sup> There is also an alternative formulation with ten elements which is also very commonly taught in the early texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucknell1984_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bucknell1984-373">[341]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Gethin, another common summary of the path to awakening wisely used in the early texts is "abandoning the <a href="/wiki/Five_hindrances" title="Five hindrances">hindrances</a>, practice of the four establishments of mindfulness and development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bojjhangas" title="Bojjhangas">the awakening factors</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin1998217–218_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin1998217–218-374">[342]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early texts also contain many different presentations of the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_paths_to_liberation#Early_Buddhism" title="Buddhist paths to liberation">Buddha's path to liberation</a> aside from the Eightfold Path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucknell1984_373-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bucknell1984-373">[341]</a></sup> According to Rupert Gethin, in the Nikayas and Agamas, the Buddha's path is mainly presented in a cumulative and gradual "step by step" process, such as that outlined in the <i><a href="/wiki/Sama%C3%B1%C3%B1aphala_Sutta" title="Samaññaphala Sutta">Samaññaphala Sutta</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199883,_165_375-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199883,_165-375">[343]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[note 33]</a></sup> Early texts that outline the graduated path include the <i>Cula-Hatthipadopama-sutta</i> (MN 27, with Chinese parallel at MĀ 146) and the <i>Tevijja Sutta</i> (DN 13, with Chinese parallel at DĀ 26 and a fragmentary Sanskrit parallel entitled the <i>Vāsiṣṭha-sūtra</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucknell1984_373-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bucknell1984-373">[341]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011189_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2011189-378">[345]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2015_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2015-379">[346]</a></sup> Other early texts like the <i>Upanisa sutta</i> (SN 12.23), present the path as reversions of the process of Dependent Origination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[347]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[note 34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some common practices which are shared by most of these early presentations of the path include <i><a href="/wiki/Buddhist_ethics" title="Buddhist ethics">sila</a></i> (ethical training), restraint of the senses (<i>indriyasamvara</i>), mindfulness and clear awareness (<i><a href="/wiki/Sati_(Buddhism)" title="Sati (Buddhism)">sati</a></i>-<i><a href="/wiki/Sampaja%C3%B1%C3%B1a" title="Sampajañña">sampajañña</a></i>) and the practice of <i>jhana</i> (the cumulative development of wholesome states).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucknell1984_373-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bucknell1984-373">[341]</a></sup> Mental development (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citta" title="Citta">citta</a> <a href="/wiki/Bhavana" title="Bhavana">bhāvanā</a></i>) was central to the Buddha's spiritual path as depicted in the earliest texts and this included <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation#Pre-sectarian_Buddhism" title="Buddhist meditation">meditative practices</a>.
</p><p>Regarding the training of right view and sense restraint, the Buddha taught that it was important to reflect on the dangers or drawbacks (<i>adinava</i>) of sensual pleasures. Various suttas discuss the different drawbacks of sensuality. In the <i>Potaliya Sutta</i> (MN 54) sensual pleasures are said by the Buddha to be a cause of conflict for all humans beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005195_383-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005195-383">[349]</a></sup> They are said to be unable to satisfy one's craving, like a clean meatless bone given to a dog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005199_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005199-384">[350]</a></sup> Sensuality is also compared to a torch held against the wind, since it burns the person holding on to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005200_385-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005200-385">[351]</a></sup> According to the Buddha, there is "a delight apart from sensual pleasures, apart from unwholesome states, which surpasses even divine bliss." The Buddha thus taught that one should take delight in the higher spiritual pleasures instead of sensual pleasure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005204_386-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005204-386">[352]</a></sup> This is explained with the simile the leper, who cauterizes his skin with fire to get relief from the pain of leprosy, but after he is cured, avoids the same flames he used to enjoy before (see MN 75, <i>Magandiya Sutta</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005205_387-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBodhi2005205-387">[353]</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous scholars such as Vetter have written on the centrality of the practice of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyāna in Buddhism"><i>dhyāna</i></a> to the teaching of the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxx,_xxxv–xxxvi,_4–5_388-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxx,_xxxv–xxxvi,_4–5-388">[354]</a></sup> It is the training of the mind, commonly translated as <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation" title="Buddhist meditation">meditation</a>, to withdraw the mind from the automatic responses to sense-impressions, and leading to a "state of perfect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upekkha" title="Upekkha">equanimity</a> and awareness (<i>upekkhā-sati-parisuddhi</i>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter19885_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter19885-389">[355]</a></sup> Dhyana is preceded and supported by various aspects of the path such as seclusion and sense restraint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2017a80,_128,_135_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2017a80,_128,_135-390">[356]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important element of <i>dhyana</i> as mental training in the early texts is the practice of <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness" title="Mindfulness">mindfulness</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Sati_(Buddhism)" title="Sati (Buddhism)">sati</a></i>), which is presented using the schemas of the "Four Ways of Mindfulness" (<i><a href="/wiki/Satipatthana" title="Satipatthana">Satipatthana</a></i>, as taught in the Pali <i><a href="/wiki/Satipatthana_Sutta" title="Satipatthana Sutta">Satipatthana Sutta</a></i> and its various parallel texts) and the sixteen elements of "Mindfulness of Breath" (<i><a href="/wiki/Anapanasati" title="Anapanasati">Anapanasati</a></i>, as taught in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anapanasati_Sutta" title="Anapanasati Sutta">Anapanasati Sutta</a></i> and its various parallels).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[note 35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because getting others to practice the path was the central goal of the Buddha's message, the early texts depict the Buddha as refusing to answer <a href="/wiki/The_unanswered_questions" title="The unanswered questions">certain metaphysical questions</a> which his contemporaries were preoccupied with, (such as "is the world eternal?"). This is because he did not see these questions as being useful on the path and as not being "connected to the goal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199866_392-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199866-392">[357]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monasticism">Monasticism</span></h4>
<p>The early Buddhist texts depict the Buddha as promoting the life of a homeless and <a href="/wiki/Celibacy" title="Celibacy">celibate</a> "<i>sramana</i>", or mendicant, as the ideal way of life for the practice of the path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199885,_88_393-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199885,_88-393">[358]</a></sup> He taught that mendicants or "beggars" (<a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu"><i>bhikkhus</i></a>) were supposed to give up all possessions and to own just a begging bowl and three robes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKalupahana199228_394-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKalupahana199228-394">[359]</a></sup> As part of the Buddha's monastic discipline, they were also supposed to rely on the wider lay community for the basic necessities (mainly food, clothing, and lodging).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199885_395-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199885-395">[360]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha's teachings on monastic discipline were preserved in the various <a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">Vinaya</a> collections of the different early schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKalupahana199228_394-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKalupahana199228-394">[359]</a></sup>
</p><p>Buddhist monastics, which included both monks and nuns, were supposed to beg for their food, were not allowed to store up food or eat after noon and they were not allowed to use gold, silver or any valuables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-396"><a href="#cite_note-396">[361]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199887_397-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199887-397">[362]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Socio-political_teachings">Socio-political teachings</span></h4>
<p>The early texts depict the Buddha as giving a deflationary account of the importance of politics to human life. Politics is inevitable and is probably even necessary and helpful, but it is also a tremendous waste of time and effort, as well as being a prime temptation to allow ego to run rampant. Buddhist political theory denies that people have a moral duty to engage in politics except to a very minimal degree (pay the taxes, obey the laws, maybe vote in the elections), and it actively portrays engagement in politics and the pursuit of enlightenment as being conflicting paths in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-398"><a href="#cite_note-398">[363]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Agga%C3%B1%C3%B1a_Sutta" title="Aggañña Sutta">Aggañña Sutta</a></i>, the Buddha teaches a history of how monarchy arose which according to Matthew J. Moore is "closely analogous to a social contract." The <i>Aggañña Sutta</i> also provides a social explanation of how different classes arose, in contrast to the Vedic views on social caste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_399-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-399">[364]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other early texts like the <i>Cakkavatti-Sīhanāda Sutta</i> and the <i>Mahāsudassana Sutta</i> focus on the figure of the righteous wheel turning leader (<a href="/wiki/Chakravarti_(Sanskrit_term)" title="Chakravarti (Sanskrit term)"><i>Cakkavatti</i></a>). This ideal leader is one who promotes Dharma through his governance. He can only achieve his status through moral purity and must promote morality and Dharma to maintain his position. According to the <i>Cakkavatti-Sīhanāda Sutta</i>, the key duties of a Cakkavatti are: "establish guard, ward, and protection according to Dhamma for your own household, your troops, your nobles, and vassals, for Brahmins and householders, town and country folk, ascetics and Brahmins, for beasts and birds. let no crime prevail in your kingdom, and to those who are in need, give property."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_399-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-399">[364]</a></sup> The sutta explains the injunction to give to the needy by telling how a line of wheel-turning monarchs falls because they fail to give to the needy, and thus the kingdom falls into infighting as poverty increases, which then leads to stealing and violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-400"><a href="#cite_note-400">[note 36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>Mahāparinibbāna Sutta,</i> the Buddha outlines several principles that he promoted among the Vajjika tribal federation, which had a quasi-republican form of government. He taught them to "hold regular and frequent assemblies", live in harmony and maintain their traditions. The Buddha then goes on to promote a similar kind of republican style of government among the Buddhist Sangha, where all monks had equal rights to attend open meetings and there would be no single leader, since The Buddha also chose not to appoint one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_399-3"><a href="#cite_note-:7-399">[364]</a></sup> Some scholars have argued that this fact signals that the Buddha preferred a <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republican</a> form of government, while others disagree with this position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_399-4"><a href="#cite_note-:7-399">[364]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_views_on_the_earliest_teachings">Scholarly views on the earliest teachings</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presectarian_Buddhism" title="Presectarian Buddhism">Presectarian Buddhism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="430" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg/170px-Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg/255px-Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg/340px-Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Buddha on a coin of <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan</a> ruler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanishka_I" title="Kanishka I">Kanishka I</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 130</span> CE.</div></div></div>
<p>Numerous scholars of early Buddhism argue that most of the teachings found in the Early Buddhist texts date back to the Buddha himself. One of these is <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gombrich" title="Richard Gombrich">Richard Gombrich</a>, who argues that since the content of the earliest texts "presents such originality, intelligence, grandeur and—most relevantly—coherence...it is hard to see it as a composite work." Thus he concludes they are "the work of one genius."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2006b[httpswwwgooglecombookseditionTheravada_BuddhismKCh-AgAAQBAJgbpv1bsqthe20work20of20one20genius_21]_401-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2006b[httpswwwgooglecombookseditionTheravada_BuddhismKCh-AgAAQBAJgbpv1bsqthe20work20of20one20genius_21]-401">[365]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Harvey_(academic)" title="Peter Harvey (academic)">Peter Harvey</a> also agrees that "much" of the Pali Canon "must derive from his [the Buddha's] teachings."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[366]</a></sup> Likewise, <a href="/wiki/A._K._Warder" title="A. K. Warder">A. K. Warder</a> has written that "there is no evidence to suggest that it [the shared teaching of the early schools] was formulated by anyone other than the Buddha and his immediate followers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder2000inside_flap_291-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder2000inside_flap-291">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, Alexander Wynne argues that "the internal evidence of the early Buddhist literature proves its historical authenticity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[367]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, other scholars of Buddhist studies have disagreed with the mostly positive view that the early Buddhist texts reflect the teachings of the historical Buddha. For example, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Conze" title="Edward Conze">Edward Conze</a> argued that the attempts of European scholars to reconstruct the original teachings of the Buddha were "all mere guesswork."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-404"><a href="#cite_note-404">[368]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other scholars argue that some teachings contained in the early texts are the authentic teachings of the Buddha, but not others. For example, according to Tilmann Vetter, the earliest core of the Buddhist teachings is the meditative practice of <i>dhyāna</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxx,_xxxv–xxxvi,_4–5_388-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxx,_xxxv–xxxvi,_4–5-388">[354]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-406"><a href="#cite_note-406">[note 37]</a></sup> Vetter argues that "liberating insight" became an essential feature of the Buddhist tradition at a later date. He posits that the Fourth Noble Truths, the Eightfold path and Dependent Origination, which are commonly seen as essential to Buddhism, are later formulations which form part of the explanatory framework of this "liberating insight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxxiv–xxxvii_407-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxxiv–xxxvii-407">[370]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lambert_Schmithausen" title="Lambert Schmithausen">Lambert Schmithausen</a> similarly argues that the mention of the four noble truths as constituting "liberating insight", which is attained after mastering the four <i>dhyānas</i>, is a later addition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981-287">[266]</a></sup> Also, according to <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bronkhorst" title="Johannes Bronkhorst">Johannes Bronkhorst</a>, the four truths may not have been formulated in earliest Buddhism, and did not serve in earliest Buddhism as a description of "liberating insight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993107_408-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993107-408">[371]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physical_characteristics_of_the_Buddha" title="Physical characteristics of the Buddha">Physical characteristics of the Buddha</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1151" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg/220px-Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg/330px-Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg/440px-Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mahapragya_exiled_1926_wk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buddhist monks from Nepal. According to the earliest sources, the Buddha looked like a typical shaved man from northeast India.</div></div></div>
<p>Early sources depict the Buddha's as similar to other Buddhist monks. Various discourses describe how he "cut off his hair and beard" when renouncing the world. Likewise, Digha Nikaya 3 has a Brahmin describe the Buddha as a shaved or bald (<i>mundaka</i>) man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-409"><a href="#cite_note-409">[372]</a></sup> Digha Nikaya 2 also describes how king Ajatashatru is unable to tell which of the monks is the Buddha when approaching the sangha and must ask his minister to point him out. Likewise, in MN 140, a mendicant who sees himself as a follower of the Buddha meets the Buddha in person but is unable to recognize him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-410"><a href="#cite_note-410">[373]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha is also described as being handsome and with a clear complexion (Digha I:115; Anguttara I:181), at least in his youth. In old age, however, he is described as having a stooped body, with slack and wrinkled limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhammikan.d.23–24_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhammikan.d.23–24-411">[374]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various Buddhist texts attribute to the Buddha a series of extraordinary physical characteristics, known as "the <a href="/wiki/Physical_characteristics_of_the_Buddha#32_Signs_of_a_Great_Man" title="Physical characteristics of the Buddha">32 Signs of the Great Man</a>" (Skt. <i>mahāpuruṣa lakṣaṇa</i>).
</p><p>According to Anālayo, when they first appear in the Buddhist texts, these physical marks were initially held to be imperceptible to the ordinary person, and required special training to detect. Later though, they are depicted as being visible by regular people and as inspiring faith in the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2017b137–138_412-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnālayo2017b137–138-412">[375]</a></sup>
</p><p>These characteristics are described in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digha_Nikaya" title="Digha Nikaya">Digha Nikaya</a>'s <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Lakkhaṇa Sutta</i></span></i> (D, I:142).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalshe1995441–460_413-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalshe1995441–460-413">[376]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_other_religions">In other religions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha_in_world_religions" title="Gautama Buddha in world religions">Gautama Buddha in world religions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu,_from_Sunari,_Medieval_period.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2147" data-file-width="1249" decoding="async" height="378" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu%2C_from_Sunari%2C_Medieval_period.jpg/220px-Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu%2C_from_Sunari%2C_Medieval_period.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu%2C_from_Sunari%2C_Medieval_period.jpg/330px-Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu%2C_from_Sunari%2C_Medieval_period.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu%2C_from_Sunari%2C_Medieval_period.jpg/440px-Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu%2C_from_Sunari%2C_Medieval_period.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_incarnation_of_Vishnu,_from_Sunari,_Medieval_period.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Buddha incarnation of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Sunari" title="Sunari">Sunari</a>, Medieval period. <a href="/wiki/Gujari_Mahal_Archaeological_Museum" title="Gujari Mahal Archaeological Museum">Gujari Mahal Archaeological Museum</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha_in_Hinduism" title="Gautama Buddha in Hinduism">Gautama Buddha in Hinduism</a></div>
<p>This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_synthesis" title="Hindu synthesis">Hindu synthesis</a> emerged after the lifetime of the Buddha, between 500<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel201312_414-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel201312-414">[377]</a></sup>–200<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarson1995_415-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarson1995-415">[378]</a></sup> BCE and c. 300 CE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel201312_414-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel201312-414">[377]</a></sup> under the pressure of the success of Buddhism and Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_Nath200121_416-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_Nath200121-416">[379]</a></sup> In response to the success of Buddhism, Gautama also came to be regarded as the 9th <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google260_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-google260-137">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGopal199073_417-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGopal199073-417">[380]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoniger1993243_418-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoniger1993243-418">[381]</a></sup> Many Hindus claim that Buddha was Hindu and cite a belief that the Buddha is the ninth avatar of Vishnu in support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-balarama_420-0"><a href="#cite_note-balarama-420">[note 38]</a></sup> The adoption of the Buddha as an incarnation began at approximately the same time as Hinduism began to predominate and Buddhism to decline in India, the co-option into a list of avatars seen to be an aspect of Hindu efforts to decisively weaken Buddhist power and appeal in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varieties-religious_421-0"><a href="#cite_note-varieties-religious-421">[383]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-422"><a href="#cite_note-422">[384]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Buddha's teachings deny the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>-<a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Atman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_423-0"><a href="#cite_note-sep-423">[385]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-424"><a href="#cite_note-424">[386]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-425"><a href="#cite_note-425">[387]</a></sup> Consequently, Buddhism is generally classified as a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nastika" title="Nastika">nāstika</a></i> school (heterodox, literally "It is not so"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-428"><a href="#cite_note-428">[note 39]</a></sup>) in contrast to the six orthodox schools of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-429"><a href="#cite_note-429">[390]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-430"><a href="#cite_note-430">[391]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996231–232_431-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996231–232-431">[392]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h3>
<p>Islamic prophet <a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Kifl" title="Dhu al-Kifl">Dhu al-Kifl</a> has been identified with the Buddha based on <a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">Surah</a> 95:1 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>, which references a fig tree – a symbol that does not feature prominently in the lives of any of the other prophets mentioned in the Qur'an. It has meanwhile been suggested that the name <i>Al-Kifl</i> could be a reference to <a href="/wiki/Kapilavastu_(ancient_city)" title="Kapilavastu (ancient city)">Kapilavastu</a>, the home of Siddartha Gautama as a boy. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYusuf2009376_432-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYusuf2009376-432">[393]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Islam" title="Classical Islam">Classical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunnism" title="Sunnism">Sunni scholar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabari" title="Tabari">Tabari</a> reports that Buddhist idols were brought from <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> in the ninth century. Such idols had been sold in Buddhist temples next to a mosque in <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>, but he does not further discuss the role of Buddha. According to the works on Buddhism by <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> (973–after 1050), views regarding the exact identity of Buddha were diverse. Accordingly, some regarded him as the divine incarnate, others as an apostle of the angels or as an <a href="/wiki/Ifrit" title="Ifrit">Ifrit</a> and others as an apostle of God sent to the human race. By the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Al-Shahrastani" title="Al-Shahrastani">al-Shahrastani</a> even compared Buddha to <a href="/wiki/Khidr" title="Khidr">Khidr</a>, described as an ideal human. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Nadim" title="Ibn Nadim">Ibn Nadim</a>, who was also familiar with <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaean teachings</a>, even identifies Buddha as a prophet, who taught a religion to "banish <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">Satan</a>", although he does not mention it explicitly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-433"><a href="#cite_note-433">[394]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddha is also regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> by the minority <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islam_and_the_Ahmadiyya_jamaʻat_434-0"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_and_the_Ahmadiyya_jamaʻat-434">[395]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_and_Christianity" title="Buddhism and Christianity">Buddhism and Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_influences_on_Christianity" title="Buddhist influences on Christianity">Buddhist influences on Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Buddhism_and_Christianity" title="Comparison of Buddhism and Christianity">Comparison of Buddhism and Christianity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="880" decoding="async" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG/220px-Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG/330px-Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG/440px-Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christ_et_Buddha_by_Paul_Ranson_1880.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Christ and Buddha</i> by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ranson" title="Paul Ranson">Paul Ranson</a>, 1880</div></div></div>
<p>The Christian <a href="/wiki/Barlaam_and_Josaphat" title="Barlaam and Josaphat">Saint Josaphat</a> is based on the Buddha. The name comes from the Sanskrit <i><a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattva</a></i> via Arabic <i>Būdhasaf</i> and Georgian <i>Iodasaph</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacdonnel1900_435-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacdonnel1900-435">[396]</a></sup> The only story in which St. Josaphat appears, <i>Barlaam and Josaphat</i>, is based on the life of the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMershman1913_436-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMershman1913-436">[397]</a></sup> Josaphat was included in earlier editions of the Roman Martyrology (feast-day 27 November)—though not in the Roman Missal—and in the Eastern Orthodox Church liturgical calendar (26 August).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_other_religions_2">In other religions</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a>, Buddha is regarded as one of the <a href="/wiki/Manifestation_of_God_(Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith)" title="Manifestation of God (Baháʼí Faith)">Manifestations of God</a>.
</p><p>Some early Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist" title="Taoist">Taoist</a>-Buddhists thought the Buddha to be a reincarnation of <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett1986_437-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett1986-437">[398]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the ancient <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnostic</a> sect of <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, the Buddha is listed among the prophets who preached the word of God before <a href="/wiki/Mani_(prophet)" title="Mani (prophet)">Mani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-438"><a href="#cite_note-438">[399]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, Buddha is mentioned as the 23rd avatar of Vishnu in the <a href="/wiki/Chaubis_Avtar" title="Chaubis Avtar">Chaubis Avtar</a>, a composition in Dasam Granth traditionally and historically attributed to <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-439"><a href="#cite_note-439">[400]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_depictions">Artistic depictions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddha_in_art" title="Buddha in art">Buddha in art</a></div>
<p>The earliest artistic depictions of the Buddha found at <a href="/wiki/Bharhut" title="Bharhut">Bharhut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a> are <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Buddhism" title="Aniconism in Buddhism">aniconic</a> and symbolic. During this early aniconic period, the Buddha is depicted by other objects or symbols, such as an empty throne, a riderless horse, footprints, a <a href="/wiki/Dharmachakra" title="Dharmachakra">Dharma wheel</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Bodhi_Tree" title="Bodhi Tree">Bodhi tree</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeidy200815_440-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeidy200815-440">[401]</a></sup> Since aniconism precludes single devotional figures, most representations are of <a href="/wiki/Life_of_Buddha_in_art" title="Life of Buddha in art">narrative scenes from his life</a>. These continued to be very important after the Buddha's person could be shown, alongside larger statues.  The art at Sanchi also depicts <a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales">Jataka tales</a>, narratives of the Buddha in his past lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeidy200819_441-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeidy200819-441">[402]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other styles of Indian Buddhist art depict the Buddha in human form, either standing, sitting crossed legged (often in the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_position" title="Lotus position">Lotus Pose</a>) or lying down on one side. Iconic representations of the Buddha became particularly popular and widespread after the first century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeidy200831_442-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeidy200831-442">[403]</a></sup> Some of these depictions, particularly those of <a href="/wiki/Gandharan_Buddhism" title="Gandharan Buddhism">Gandharan Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Central_Asia" title="Buddhism in Central Asia">Central Asian Buddhism</a>, were influenced by <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic art</a>, a style known as <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall19601–40_443-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall19601–40-443">[404]</a></sup>  The subsequently influenced the art of <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhism" title="East Asian Buddhism">East Asian Buddhist</a> images, as well as those of Southeast Asian Theravada Buddhism.
</p>
